6 min
Analogy of Creator with Other Attributes
Maturidi defends the temporality of creation by analogy with other attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses an analogy between the attributes of Creator and other attributes like Knowing to argue that 'Creator' does not imply eternal creation.

Core Idea
The attribute 'Creator' does not imply eternal creation, just as 'Knowing' does not imply eternal objects.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology defending the temporality of creation. By drawing an analogy with other divine attributes, the author shows that the relationship between attribute and object does not entail the eternal existence of the object. Let's unpack the reasoning from the original Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
The Arabic is dense; note the key terms 'خالق', 'سميع', 'عليم'.
2
Identify the analogy
Notice how the author compares 'Creator' to 'Hearing, Knowing': just as these attributes do not necessitate eternal objects, similarly 'Creator' does not necessitate eternal creation.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that the attribute 'Creator' is analogous to other attributes and does not require an eternal creation. The author also notes that definite articles and common usage support this.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ليس في قوله الخالق ذلك'.
4
Reflect on the implication
This argument refutes the idea that if God is Creator, then creation must be eternal. Ask yourself how this preserves God's transcendence while affirming temporal creation.
This connects to broader kalām discussions on God's attributes and the world's temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إذ لم يجب في القول بسميع عليم كون كل معلوم مقدور عليه مسموع في الأزل ، فمثله في القول بالخالق ، لكن خالق الأشياء ليكون على ما هي كما علمها كذلك وقادر ونحو ذلك . وإذا كان القول بعالم سميع بصير وبالعالم السميع البصير واحدا فكذلك بتخالق والخالق واحد ، بل الخالق في إيجاب قدم الخلق أحق لو كان التقدير من الملفوظ من خالق ، ويرى يدخل في ذلك أنه على وزن خالق يقال : مالك يوم الدين ، وخالق كل شيء كل حادث وقائم ، ليس في قوله الخالق ذلك ، ولا هو يقول عليه في العرف، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then, since it is not necessitated in the saying 'hearing, knowing' that every known, powerful, or heard thing exist in eternity, similarly in the saying 'the Creator'; rather, 'Creator of things' means He is such that things are as He knows them, just as He is powerful and so on. And if the saying 'knowing, hearing, seeing' and 'the knowing, the hearing, the seeing' are the same, then likewise 'the Creator' and 'Creator' are one. Rather, the term 'the Creator' would be more deserving of implying the eternity of creation if the implication were derived from the wording of 'Creator'. And one sees that it includes that it is on the pattern of 'Creator' as is said: 'Master of the Day of Judgment' and 'Creator of everything' – everything that is originated and subsisting. That is not in His saying 'the Creator', nor is it said in common usage. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage argues by analogy that the attribute 'Creator' does not imply that the created things are eternal. Just as saying God is 'Hearing, Knowing' does not mean that all known and heard things exist eternally (since objects of knowledge are temporal), similarly saying 'Creator' does not imply that the creation is eternal. The point is that attributes like 'Knowing' and 'Creator' describe God's relation to things without eternalizing those things. The author then notes that 'Creator' and 'the Creator' are essentially the same. He adds that if the word 'Creator' were taken to imply the eternity of creation, it would be more so when used as 'the Creator' (with the definite article), but this is not the case. He concludes by citing examples like 'Master of the Day of Judgment' and 'Creator of everything' – these do not imply that the Day of Judgment or everything always existed. This refutes the idea that 'Creator' requires eternal creation.

Key Terms
خالق
سميع
عليم
أزل
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attribute 'Creator' implies that creation is eternal.
Correction: The passage argues that just as 'Hearing, Knowing' do not require eternal objects, 'Creator' does not require eternal creation.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the author use to argue that 'Creator' does not imply eternal creation?
A) The analogy between 'Creator' and 'King'.
B) The analogy between 'Creator' and 'Hearing, Knowing'.
C) The analogy between 'Creator' and 'Powerful'.
D) The analogy between 'Creator' and 'Master of the Day of Judgment'.

Key Takeaway
The attribute 'Creator' does not necessitate an eternal creation, just as 'Knowing' does not necessitate eternal objects of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's attribute 'Creator' does not force creation to be eternal, what does that mean for how you view your own existence? Are you comfortable being a temporal being created by an eternal God, or do you secretly wish to be self-sufficient like Him? How does this truth shape your dependence on Him today?
?
The lesson argues that 'Knowing' does not require eternal known things. Does that change how you think about God's knowledge of your sins? Does it relieve you to know that your sins are not eternal objects but temporal, and that God's attribute of forgiveness (al-Ghafūr) similarly does not eternalize your sins? How should this affect your repentance and hope for His mercy right now?
?
When you call God 'al-Khāliq' (the Creator), are you acknowledging your own temporality and complete reliance on Him? Will you consciously pause and reflect on this meaning the next time you say 'al-Khāliq' in your prayers, letting it humble you and deepen your trust in His creative power?

Why Negative Descriptions Alone Cannot Describe God
Maturidi argues negative descriptions alone insufficient for knowing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that merely negating imperfections does not provide positive knowledge of God's attributes.

Core Idea
Negating imperfections does not teach us what God is, only what He is not.

Maturidi opens a crucial discussion on how we describe God. Many assume that saying 'God is not ignorant' tells us something about His knowledge, but Maturidi points out the logical gap: negation alone fails to affirm any positive attribute. This passage challenges us to think deeper about the language we use for the Divine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical flow Maturidi employs.
Pay attention to the examples: not ignorant, not incapable, not deaf.
2
Identify the key examples
Maturidi lists negative descriptions and contrasts them with positive ones. List each negation and its corresponding positive attribute.
The positive attributes are powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing.
3
Understand the logical gap
Why does Maturidi say that 'not ignorant' does not imply 'knowing'? Consider that a stone is not ignorant but also not knowing. This shows the gap.
4
Examine the alternative proposal
Maturidi suggests if one only intends to negate opposites, one could say 'He is sound, safe, healthy' – which are also negative. Why is that unacceptable?
5
Draw the conclusion
Since the alternative is not permissible, the original claim that negations alone are sufficient fails. Reflect on why.
This shows that positive attributes must be affirmed, not just negatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ قد يجوز أن يقال للشيء [ليس] بجاهل ولا عاجز ولا أصم ، ولا يجب به الوصف بقادر عالم سميع بصير ، فإذا لم يكن فى ذا سوى نفى الذي ذكر فحرف النفي أقرب من حرف يفهم ما لا منفعة في فهمه ، بل فيه كل ضرر ، ولو لم يرد بذلك سوى نفى الأضداد فليقل : هو صحيح سليم معافى على نفى الأضداد دون تحقيق الذي ذكر . فإذا لم يجز ذا بان أن الذى زعم من بيان حاصل الذي ذكر
For it may be said of a thing [that it is] not ignorant, not incapable, not deaf, and it is not obligatory thereby to describe it as powerful, knowing, hearing, seeing. So if in this there is only negation of what was mentioned, then the particle of negation is closer than a particle that conveys something of which there is no benefit in understanding—rather in it there is all harm. And if by that he only intended negation of opposites, let him say: He is sound, safe, healthy, on the negation of opposites without affirming what was mentioned. So if this is not permissible, it becomes clear that what he claimed regarding the explanation of the essence of what was mentioned [is false].
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage defines a problem: merely saying God is 'not ignorant, not incapable, not deaf' does not imply positive attributes like power, knowledge, hearing, seeing. Maturidi argues that using such negations is insufficient for meaningful knowledge of God. He points out that if the only purpose is to negate opposites, one could simply say 'He is sound, safe, healthy'—pure negations without positive content. Since this is not acceptable for describing God, it shows that the method of only negating opposites fails to give a true account of God's attributes.

Key Terms
نفي
صفات
أضداد
جاهل
عاجز
أصم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating imperfections (e.g., 'God is not ignorant') gives positive knowledge of God's attributes.
Correction: Maturidi argues that negations alone do not imply positive attributes, as shown by the example of a stone which also is not ignorant but lacks knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject purely negative descriptions of God?
A) Because they imply God has imperfections
B) Because they do not convey positive knowledge of God's attributes
C) Because they are forbidden in the Quran
D) Because they lead to anthropomorphism

Key Takeaway
Negative descriptions alone cannot provide meaningful knowledge of God's attributes; they must be accompanied by positive affirmations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you mostly dwell on what He is not (not limited, not like us) rather than actively affirming His specific positive attributes like His power, knowledge, and mercy? How might this shift your worship if you focused on one positive attribute each day this week?
?
If a non-Muslim asked you 'What is God like?', would your answer lean on negatives ('He is not unjust, not weak') or would you confidently state positives ('He is All-Powerful, All-Knowing, All-Merciful')? Commit to memorizing and reflecting on one of His positive names this month.
?
Are you content with a vague belief that 'God is great' without specifying what that greatness means? What specific attribute of Allah will you praise Him for in your next prayer, and how will it affect your actions?

Orderly Acts Prove God's Knowledge and Power
Maturidi presents the proof that orderly acts directly indicate God's knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the orderly emergence of consecutive acts in the world directly indicates the agent's knowledge and power, not merely that He lacks ignorance and weakness.

Core Idea
Orderly acts are direct evidence of knowledge and power, not just of not being ignorant.

Maturidi begins by establishing that the well-ordered, consecutive acts we observe in creation are themselves proof of the agent's knowledge and power over them. He distinguishes this from the weaker claim that they only show the agent is not ignorant or weak. This distinction is crucial for affirming positive attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the phrase 'هي العلم بها والقدرة عليها' — this is the key claim.
2
Identify the contrast
Maturidi contrasts two interpretations: (1) the acts are proof of knowledge and power, vs. (2) they are merely proof that the agent is not ignorant or weak. Which does he affirm?
He affirms the positive attribution.
3
Understand the example of accidents
He says that no one described solely by negative traits can produce any act or consistency, as with accidents. This illustrates that positive attributes are necessary for orderly action.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why is it important to affirm that Allah has knowledge and power, not just that He lacks ignorance?
This connects to broader kalam discussions about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن خروج الأفعال المتتابعة على حسن النظام والإحكام هي العلم بها والقدرة عليها لا أنها أدلة من ليس بجاهل ولا عاجز ؛ إذ غير واحد بما وصفه لا يكون منه فعل البته ولا اتساق ، نحو الأعراض كلها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنها أسماء عن صفات تسقط لسقوط الصفات ، فإذ لم تحقق الصفات صيرت الأسماء أسماء ألقاب ، وإذا صارت كذا فالقول بأنه لم يزل كذا كلام لا معنى له لإحالة اللقب فى الأزل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم إذ لم يجب في القول بسميع عليم كون كل معلوم مقدور عليه مسموع فى الأزل ، فمثله في القول بالخالق ، لكن خالق الأشياء ليكون على ما على ما هی کما ده عالم بها كذلك وقادر ونحو ذلك . وإذا كان القول بعالم سميع بصير وبالعالم السميع البصير واحدا فكذلك بتخالق والخالق / واحد ، بل الخالق في إيجاب قدم [٦٤ ب الخلق أحق لو كان التقدير من الملفوظ من خالق ،، بری يدخل في ذلك أنه على وزن خالق يقال : مالك يوم الدين ، وخالق كل شيء كل حادث وقائم ، ليس في قوله الخالق ذلك ، ولا هو يقول عليه في العرف، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And further, the emergence of consecutive acts in good order and perfection is knowledge of them and power over them, not that they are proofs of one who is not ignorant and not incapable; for not one who is described by what he is described has any act at all or consistency, like all accidents. And there is no power except with God. Moreover, they are names derived from attributes that fall away with the falling away of attributes. So when the attributes are not realized, the names become mere titles. And when they become so, the statement that He has always been such is meaningless because a title cannot be eternal. And there is no power except with God. Then, since it is not necessary in saying 'Hearing, Knowing' that everything known is possible and heard in pre-eternity, likewise in saying 'the Creator'. But the Creator of things is such that He knows them as they are, and has power over them, and so forth. And if the statement 'Knowing, Hearing, Seeing' and 'the Knowing, the Hearing, the Seeing' are one, so too are 'the Creator' and 'Creator' one. Nay, the Creator in necessitating the pre-eternity [of creation] is more entitled—if the estimation is based on the word 'Creator'—it would enter into that that he is on the measure of 'Creator' and it is said: 'Master of the Day of Judgment' and 'Creator of everything, every thing that is temporal and subsisting', but this is not in His saying 'the Creator', nor does He say it according to custom. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage presents a proof: the orderly emergence of acts in the world demonstrates the agent's knowledge and power, not merely that He is not ignorant or weak. Maturidi then discusses that names derive from attributes; if attributes are denied, names become mere titles, and calling God eternal by such titles is meaningless. He addresses the names 'Hearing', 'Knowing', and 'Creator', arguing that just as 'Knowing' does not imply that everything known is eternal, similarly 'Creator' does not imply creation is eternal. The grammatical form 'Creator' is like 'Master' and does not necessitate pre-eternity of the object. Thus, the name al-Khaliq does not imply the pre-eternity of creation.

Key Terms
خروج الأفعال
النظام
الإحكام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The orderly acts in the world only prove that the agent is not ignorant or weak.
Correction: Maturidi argues that they directly indicate the agent's knowledge and power over them, not just the absence of opposites.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, the orderly emergence of consecutive acts is evidence of what?
A) That the agent is not ignorant or weak
B) That the agent has knowledge and power over them
C) That the acts are self-caused
D) That the agent is eternal

Key Takeaway
The well-ordered nature of consecutive acts in creation directly proves that their agent possesses both knowledge and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that the very order you witness in the universe—your heartbeat, the sunrise, the return of seasons—is not a neutral fact but a direct testimony to God's knowledge and power. When was the last time you paused to see that order as a personal message from your Creator, and how did you respond?
?
Have you ever thought that it is enough to say 'God is not ignorant' or 'God is not weak' to explain the world's order? What would it mean for your worship if you truly internalized that the order around you proves that God knows you intimately and has power over every detail of your life?
?
If you truly believe that every orderly act in creation is a product of God's knowledge and power, then where in your life do you trust your own plans more than His order? Will you surrender one specific area today, acknowledging that His knowledge and power are superior to your own?

Names Derive from Attributes: The Case of 'Creator'
Maturidi discusses the derivation of names from attributes and refutes that 'Creator' implies pre-eternal creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine names derive from attributes, and that the name 'Creator' (al-Khaliq) does not imply that creation is eternal.

Core Idea
If attributes are denied, names become meaningless titles; 'Creator' does not necessitate eternal creation.

Maturidi continues by discussing the relationship between divine names and attributes. He argues that names are derived from attributes; if attributes are not affirmed, the names become mere titles, and it is meaningless to say God has always been such a title. He then applies this to the name 'Creator', showing that just as 'Knowing' does not imply that all known things are eternal, 'Creator' does not imply that creation is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle: names derive from attributes
Maturidi states that names come from attributes. If attributes are not realized, names become mere titles (ألقاب). This is important for understanding divine names.
2
The problem with titles
If a name is just a title, saying God has always been that title is meaningless because a title cannot be eternal. This shows why we must affirm real attributes.
3
Apply to 'Creator'
Maturidi argues that just as the name 'Knowing' does not imply that all known things are eternal, 'Creator' does not imply that creation is eternal.
4
Grammatical argument
He notes that the form 'Creator' (خالق) is like 'Master' (مالك) and does not necessitate the pre-eternity of its object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن خروج الأفعال المتتابعة على حسن النظام والإحكام هي العلم بها والقدرة عليها لا أنها أدلة من ليس بجاهل ولا عاجز ؛ إذ غير واحد بما وصفه لا يكون منه فعل البته ولا اتساق ، نحو الأعراض كلها ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . على أنها أسماء عن صفات تسقط لسقوط الصفات ، فإذ لم تحقق الصفات صيرت الأسماء أسماء ألقاب ، وإذا صارت كذا فالقول بأنه لم يزل كذا كلام لا معنى له لإحالة اللقب فى الأزل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم إذ لم يجب في القول بسميع عليم كون كل معلوم مقدور عليه مسموع فى الأزل ، فمثله في القول بالخالق ، لكن خالق الأشياء ليكون على ما على ما هی کما ده عالم بها كذلك وقادر ونحو ذلك . وإذا كان القول بعالم سميع بصير وبالعالم السميع البصير واحدا فكذلك بتخالق والخالق / واحد ، بل الخالق في إيجاب قدم [٦٤ ب الخلق أحق لو كان التقدير من الملفوظ من خالق ،، بری يدخل في ذلك أنه على وزن خالق يقال : مالك يوم الدين ، وخالق كل شيء كل حادث وقائم ، ليس في قوله الخالق ذلك ، ولا هو يقول عليه في العرف، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And further, the emergence of consecutive acts in good order and perfection is knowledge of them and power over them, not that they are proofs of one who is not ignorant and not incapable; for not one who is described by what he is described has any act at all or consistency, like all accidents. And there is no power except with God. Moreover, they are names derived from attributes that fall away with the falling away of attributes. So when the attributes are not realized, the names become mere titles. And when they become so, the statement that He has always been such is meaningless because a title cannot be eternal. And there is no power except with God. Then, since it is not necessary in saying 'Hearing, Knowing' that everything known is possible and heard in pre-eternity, likewise in saying 'the Creator'. But the Creator of things is such that He knows them as they are, and has power over them, and so forth. And if the statement 'Knowing, Hearing, Seeing' and 'the Knowing, the Hearing, the Seeing' are one, so too are 'the Creator' and 'Creator' one. Nay, the Creator in necessitating the pre-eternity [of creation] is more entitled—if the estimation is based on the word 'Creator'—it would enter into that that he is on the measure of 'Creator' and it is said: 'Master of the Day of Judgment' and 'Creator of everything, every thing that is temporal and subsisting', but this is not in His saying 'the Creator', nor does He say it according to custom. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage presents a proof: the orderly emergence of acts in the world demonstrates the agent's knowledge and power, not merely that He is not ignorant or weak. Maturidi then discusses that names derive from attributes; if attributes are denied, names become mere titles, and calling God eternal by such titles is meaningless. He addresses the names 'Hearing', 'Knowing', and 'Creator', arguing that just as 'Knowing' does not imply that everything known is eternal, similarly 'Creator' does not imply creation is eternal. The grammatical form 'Creator' is like 'Master' and does not necessitate pre-eternity of the object. Thus, the name al-Khaliq does not imply the pre-eternity of creation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name 'Creator' implies that creation is eternal.
Correction: Maturidi argues that just as 'Knowing' does not imply eternality of known things, 'Creator' does not imply eternality of creation.

Checkpoint
What happens to divine names if the corresponding attributes are denied?
A) They become meaningless titles
B) They become stronger
C) They are still valid
D) They indicate eternal objects

Key Takeaway
Divine names derive from attributes; denying attributes reduces names to titles, and the name 'Creator' does not necessitate the pre-eternity of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah 'al-Khaliq' in your prayers, do you truly reflect on what that name means about His power and knowledge, or has it become a title you recite without heart? What would change if you paused and realized that His being Creator is not a passive title but a living reality that He knows and controls everything? Will you commit to pondering one divine name each day this week?
?
The passage shows that denying attributes reduces names to meaningless titles. Have you, even unknowingly, stripped God's names of their weight by failing to believe in His attributes? Where do you need to repent and restore the depth of your understanding?
?
If 'Creator' does not necessitate eternal creation, then the world is temporal and you are a new creation. How does this urgency affect your priorities? What would you do differently today knowing that the same God who created you is actively knowing and willing your actions?

Knowledge of God Through the World's Indication
Maturidi establishes that the world indicates only God's attributes, not His essence, through three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of God is only possible through the world's testimony to His attributes, not His essence, and the three rational proofs Maturidi presents.

Core Idea
The world points to God's attributes, never to His essence directly.

Before we can affirm anything about God, we must first establish how we can know Him at all. Maturidi begins with a foundational principle: since our senses and direct revelation cannot access God Himself, we must rely on the world as a sign. This passage explains exactly how the world indicates God—by testifying to His attributes (power, knowledge) through observable patterns, while never revealing His essence. It also warns against false attributions like spatial or relational qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then the literal translation. Notice the flow: principle → proof → examples → conclusion.
Pay attention to the repeated phrase 'دليل' (proof) – it structures the argument.
2
Identify the three proofs from the world
Look for the three phenomena that Maturidi presents as evidence: existence after non-existence, variation and combination of opposites in one thing, and consistent administration despite multiplicity.
Each is introduced with 'هو دليل' or similar.
3
Understand the 'attribute vs essence' distinction
Why does Maturidi insist that the world only indicates attributes, not essence? Reflect on his reasoning: nothing in the sensed world directly shows the 'what' of God, only the 'how' of His actions.
This refutes those who claim direct intuitive knowledge of God's essence.
4
Apply the principle to spatial/relational attributes
Maturidi concludes by denying that God can be described by place, connection, separation, etc., even as negations. See how this follows from the earlier rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل أن الله تعالى إذ لا سبيل إلى العلم به إلا من طريق دلالة العالم عليه ، بانقطاع وجوه الوصول إلى معرفته من طريق الحواس عليه أو شهادة السمع ، ثم الشاهد يدل عليه من وجه الشهادة له بالصفة لا من وجه الشهادة بالذات ؛ إذ الوجود بعد أن لم يكن هو دليل الإيجاد والإحداث الذي به يعلم الموجود المحدث. واختلاف أحوال الشاهد واجتماع المتضاد في الواحد هو دليل قدرته ونفاذ التدبير الذى بالمدبر القوى يكون ، واتساق التدبير وعدم التفاوت في الواقع تحت العقل على كثرته دليل علم العقل الذي به يعلم العالم ، ولا شيء فى المحسوس يدل على ذات ، إذا نفى عنه الصفة لم يجز القول باثبات ذات غير تحقيق الصفات : إذ ذلك غير طريق شهادة العيان ، وكذالك شهادة من ثبت صدقهم بالأدلة جاء بالعليم السميع البصير على ذكر العلم والقدرة ونحو ذلك ، مع العلم أن هذه الأسماء أسماء الصفات من ثم إذ لم يجز الوصف بالمكان وبالخروج أو الدخول أو الاتصال أو الانفصال أو البينونة أو نحو ذلك ، على نفى أضداد / تلك الأحوال من غير إثبات تحقيق الملفوظ ، وكذلك شأن الاجتماع والافتراق والتحرك والسكون لم يجز الذي قالوا ، ١٠ وبالله التوفيق . ر
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The principle is that since there is no way to know God Almighty except through the indication of the world upon Him, because the means of attaining knowledge of Him through the senses or the testimony of hearing are cut off. Then the witnessed indicates Him in the aspect of testifying to the attribute, not in the aspect of testifying to the essence. For existence after non-existence is the proof of origination and creation by which the originated temporal thing is known. And the variation of the states of the witnessed and the combination of opposites in one thing is the proof of His power and the effectiveness of the administration that belongs to the powerful administrator. And the consistency of administration and the absence of discrepancy in what falls under the intellect, despite its multiplicity, is the proof of the intellect's knowledge by which the knower knows the world. And there is nothing in the sensed that indicates the essence. When the attribute is negated from it, it is not permissible to affirm an essence without realizing the attributes, for that is not the path of direct testimony. And likewise, the testimony of those whose truthfulness is established by proofs came with 'the Knowing, the Hearing, the Seeing' mentioning knowledge, power, and so forth, while knowing that these names are names of attributes. Then, since it is not permissible to describe by place, by exiting or entering, by connection or separation, by distinctness or the like, on the negation of the opposites of those states without affirming the reality of what is uttered, and likewise the matter of gathering and separating, movement and stillness, that which they said is not permissible. And success is with God.
Source pages p. 728

Commentary
This passage defines the foundational principle of knowing God: it is only through the world's indication, since direct sensory or auditory knowledge is impossible. The world testifies to God's attributes, not His essence, because existence after non-existence points to a creator. The diversity of states and combination of opposites in one thing prove God's power; the consistent, discrepancy-free administration proves His knowledge. Nothing in the sensed world reveals the essence; negating attributes would make affirming the essence meaningless. Furthermore, the prophets' testimony affirms attributes like knowledge and power, and these names are names of attributes. Then Maturidi argues that spatial and relational attributes (place, entering, exiting, etc.) cannot be affirmed even by negating their opposites; one must affirm the reality of the attribute in a proper manner. Thus, the opponents' view is invalid.

Key Terms
دلالة العالم
شهادة
صفة
ذات
قدرة
علم
أسماء الصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world can give direct knowledge of God's essence, or that God can be known through sensory experience.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the world only indicates God's attributes, not His essence. Sensory paths are cut off entirely; knowledge comes only through rational inference from the world's qualities.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the world indicate about God?
A) His essence directly
B) His attributes, not His essence
C) His omnipotence only
D) His oneness only

Key Takeaway
The world's patterns—origination, variety, and order—point only to God's attributes, never to His essence, and this principle guides all proper theological discourse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world's order and changes, do you genuinely allow those signs to point you to God's attributes, or do you find yourself trying to picture His essence? What does that reveal about your trust in the limits He has set for knowing Him?
?
If God's essence is beyond direct knowledge, how do you react internally? Does it humble you to accept that you cannot fully grasp Him, or do you feel restless? What would it take for you to be content with knowing Him through His attributes alone?
?
The world testifies to God's power through opposites uniting in you. Where in your life do you experience weakness and strength, heat and cold, joy and sorrow? Do you see those as indicators of His control, or do you try to manage them yourself without acknowledging His power?

Proof of God's Eternal Knowledge from the Temporal World
Maturidi proves God's eternal knowledge from the world's temporality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves that God's knowledge is eternal and essential, not derived from the world.

Core Idea
The world's ordered temporality implies a prior, eternal knowledge.

Maturidi presents a rational argument for God's knowledge. He begins with the established fact that the world is temporal (hadith), and then shows that its orderly coherence cannot be accidental but must stem from prior knowledge. This leads to the conclusion that God knows by His essence, independently of the existence of objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Let the author's own words set the context before analyzing.
Notice the concise, logical style of Maturidi.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument starts with the temporality of the world (ḥudūth al-ʿālam). Understand why this is taken as established here.
In kalām, the temporality of the world is often proven before discussing divine attributes.
3
Trace the logical inference
Follow the reasoning: the world's orderly succession (qiyām al-awāʾil bi-l-awākhir) cannot occur without prior knowledge. This is the core step.
4
Understand the rejection of sensory derivation
Maturidi then argues that the knowledge could not have come from the world through senses or analogy, so it must be essential to God.
This eliminates the possibility that God learned from creation.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The final conclusion: God is Knowing by essence, and His knowledge does not depend on the existence of objects—it is the same whether the known is absent or present.
This is a key point in Maturidi's theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : إذ ثبت حدث العالم ، ومحال كونه بعد . أن لم يكن على ما عليه من قيام الأوائل بالأواخر واتفاق ذلك عليم أنه كان عن علم به ، ثم محال كون نحس به يعلم ولا محسوس أو قياس ولا عبرة ، ثبت أنه عالم لذاته ، وفيها العالم لذات العالم سواء [فى] غيبة المعلوم وحضرته ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Since the temporality of the world is established, and it is impossible that it be after it was not as it is—[namely] the subsistence of the firsts with the lasts and the agreement of that—it is known that it was from knowledge of it. Then it is impossible that something [like] a misfortune be known by it, and no sense or analogy is a lesson. So it is established that He is Knowing by His essence, and the knower of the world by essence is the same whether the known is absent or present. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 729

Commentary
This passage presents a proof: the world is temporal (hadith), and it is impossible that it came to be as it is—with its orderly succession and coherence—without being from knowledge. Therefore, the world was created according to prior knowledge. Since it is impossible that this knowledge came from the world itself (as it is not sensible or analogically inferred after its existence), it must be that God is knowing by His essence, and His knowledge is not dependent on the existence of the known. Thus, God knows all things equally whether they exist or not.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
علم
عالم لذاته
غيبة
حضرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge is acquired from the world after its creation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the world's order implies prior knowledge, and it is impossible for that knowledge to be derived from the world through senses or analogy after its existence, so God's knowledge is eternal and essential.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi prove that God has eternal knowledge?
A) By claiming God learned from the world
B) By arguing the world's orderly temporality implies prior knowledge
C) By analogy with human knowledge
D) By revelation alone

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is eternal and essential, not dependent on the existence of created things, as proven by the orderly temporality of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of you is complete and unchanging, what part of your life are you still trying to hide from Him? Will you expose it to Him today?
?
You rely on your senses and analogies to navigate life, but this lesson says God's knowledge is not from such means. Where in your life are you trusting your own reasoning more than God's eternal knowledge? What would it look like to surrender that area to Him?
?
The passage ends with 'lā quwwata illā billāh' (no power except with God). Is there a situation you are facing today where you are acting as if your own power is enough? How will you consciously acknowledge His power instead?

God's Knowledge is Essential and Prior
Maturidi reiterates that God's knowledge is essential, not acquired

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's knowledge is essential (bi-dhatihi) and not acquired from creation.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is self-subsisting, not learned from the world.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi reiterates a foundational principle: God's knowledge does not come from sense perception or from the world. Instead, the world itself points to God's prior and essential knowledge. Let us read carefully to follow his concise reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without stopping. Then read the full translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in before analyzing.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the starting principle
Maturidi starts by restating that God is known not through sensory perception. Why is this important? This sets the stage for the proof that follows.
3
Trace the proof from creation
Notice how he uses the fact that God created the world in a specific way—pointing to Him—as a proof that God knew the world before it existed. This is the core link.
He says: the created thing is a proof of Him, so He knew it before it came to be.
4
Conclude essential knowledge
Since God's knowledge preceded creation and there is no external source from which He learns, His knowledge must be essential (by His essence). This is the final conclusion.
The rhetorical question at the end emphasizes that God's knowledge is not through another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل فى ذلك ما ذكرت أنه عرف لا بالحس وفيما عرف به دليل علمه به ؛ إذ جعله على وجه دله عليه ، ثم لم يحتمل أن يكون يكن غير حتى أنشأه ، ثم ذلك المنشأ كان دليلا عليه ، ثبت أنه كان قبل كونه عالما به ، ولا غير له غيره به علم ، ثبت أنه عالم بذاته . علمه به غیره ؛ لما لم لا بغيره ، والله الموفق .
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The principle in that is what I mentioned: that it is known not by sense, and in what is known by it is a proof of His knowledge of it. For He made it in a manner that indicates Him. Then it is not possible that it be something other than [Him] until He created it. Then that created thing was a proof of Him, so it is established that He was knowing it before its existence. And there is no other than Him by which He knows. So it is established that He is knowing by His essence. His knowledge of it is by other than Him? Since it is not by other than Him, and God is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 729

Commentary
This passage summarizes the previous argument: God is not known through the senses, but the world is a proof of His knowledge because He created it in a way that points to Him. The world could not have existed without His prior knowledge, so He knew it before creation. Since there is no external source of knowledge for God, His knowledge is essential (bi-dhatihi). The rhetorical question 'His knowledge of it is by other than Him?' is answered: no, it is not by other than Him.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's knowledge is acquired from observing the world.
Correction: Maturidi argues that God's knowledge precedes creation and is not dependent on the world; it is essential and self-subsisting.

Checkpoint

A) Because God learned from the world
B) Because there is no external source for His knowledge
C) Because the world is eternal
D) Because the prophets said so

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is not derived from creation; it is essential, prior, and independent of any external source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Maturidi argues that creation is a proof of God's knowledge. When you look at the created world, do you see it as a sign of God's omniscience, or do you take it for granted? What one change in your daily observation could remind you of God's prior knowledge?

Proof that God is Knowing by His Essence
Maturidi proves God's knowledge from the order and harmony of creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's knowledge is essential, based on the harmony and order of creation.

Core Idea
The harmony of creation proves God's essential knowledge, not derived from sensation.

Maturidi uses the observable order in the world to demonstrate that the Creator must have knowledge by His own essence. The argument assumes the world is temporary (hadith) and then shows that its intricate design points to a knowing creator. This passage lays out that reasoning, concluding that God's knowledge is not acquired but inherent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The argument begins by establishing that the world is temporary—it came into existence after not existing. This is a foundational assumption.
The temporality of the world is not argued here but taken as given.
2
Observe the harmony
Notice how the world is ordered: earlier things support later ones in a consistent, harmonious way. This is not random.
The phrase 'قيام الأوائل بالأواخر' refers to this interdependence.
3
Draw the inference
Such order indicates knowledge behind the creation. Since no sensible object, analogy, or lesson can be the source of this knowledge, it must be from the Creator's own essence.
The Arabic 'محال كون نحس به يعلم ولا محسوس أو قياس أو عبرة' eliminates external sources.
4
Reinforce the essential nature
The second paragraph emphasizes that God is known not through sensation but through His creation, which itself is evidence of His pre-existing knowledge. He knew creation before it existed.
This confirms that His knowledge is not acquired but essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : إذ ثبت حدث العالم، ومحال كونه بعد أن لم يكن على ما عليه من قيام الأوائل بالأواخر واتفاق ذلك عليم أنه كان عن علم به، ثم محال كون نحس به يعلم ولا محسوس أو قياس أو عبرة، ثبت أنه عالم لذاته، وفيها العالم لذات العالم سواء [فى] غيبة المعلوم وحضرته، ولا قوة إلا بالله. قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل في ذلك ما ذكرت أنه عرف لا بالحس وفيما عرف به دليل علمه به؛ إذ جعله على وجه دله عليه، ثم لم يحتمل أن يكون يكن غير حتى أنشأه، ثم ذلك المنشأ كان دليلا عليه، ثبت أنه كان قبل كونه عالما به، ولا غير له غيره به علم، ثبت أنه عالم بذاته.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Since the temporality of the world is established, and it is impossible for it to be after it was not upon the state it is of the establishment of the firsts upon the lasts and the harmony of that, it is known that it was by knowledge of it. Then, it is impossible for it to be something by which He knows while there is no sensible or analogy or lesson, it is established that He is knowing by His essence. And in it, the knower of the essence of the world is the same in the absence and presence of the known. And there is no power except with God. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The foundation in that is what I mentioned: that He is known not by sensation, and in what He is known by is evidence of His knowledge of it, since He made it in a way that indicates it. Then, it is not possible that it was something other until He brought it into existence, then that brought-into-existence was evidence upon Him, it is established that He was before its coming-into-being knowing it, and there is no other for Him by which He knows, it is established that He is knowing by His essence.
Source pages p. 729

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for God's essential knowledge. It begins by assuming the temporality of the world (that it came into existence after not existing). Given the world's orderly structure, where earlier things support later ones in harmony, this indicates that it was created with knowledge. Since knowledge cannot be derived from sense perception or analogy in this case, God must be knowing by His own essence. The second statement reinforces that God is known not through sensation but through His creation, which serves as evidence of His knowledge. Since the creation was brought into existence from nothing, God knew it before it existed, and He has no other source of knowledge, thus He is knowing by His essence.

Key Terms

علم
عالم لذاته
قياس
حس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's knowledge is acquired from creation.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the orderly creation is evidence that God already has knowledge by His essence, not that He learns from it.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the evidence that God is knowing by His essence?
A) The world's temporality alone.
B) The orderly harmony of creation and the impossibility of deriving that knowledge from sensation or analogy.
C) The fact that the world is sensible.
D) The reports of prophets.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves that God's knowledge is essential by showing that the harmony of the world can only be explained by a knowing creator whose knowledge is not derived from creation.

The Sophist and the Egg: Defending Divine Omnipotence
A Sophist's question on God's ability to put the world into an egg is answered with a nuanced resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a logical paradox about divine power is resolved by distinguishing between literal and figurative intentions, thereby upholding God's omnipotence.

Core Idea
Divine omnipotence is not limited by logical contradictions in human questions.

A Sophist once posed a seemingly tricky question: can God put the entire world into an egg? At first glance, this appears to challenge God's power. But the response reveals that the question itself contains a logical flaw. This lesson unpacks the paradox, the initial contradiction, and the final distinction that protects divine omnipotence while respecting logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get the overall flow before diving into details.
2
Identify the Sophist's trap
Notice how the Sophist's question leads to a contradiction: the egg must be both larger and smaller than itself. Understand why the initial answer labels it a contradiction.
This shows that the question is not a genuine challenge but a logical puzzle.
3
Explore the faqih's two interpretations
The faqih explains that if the intention is to belittle the world while keeping the egg intact, it's impossible. But if the intention is to actually place the world into the egg, there are two sub-cases. Identify each.
The second sub-case leaves room for divine power.
4
See how God's power is defended
The second sub-case states that if the egg is expanded to contain the world, God can do that. This resolves the paradox and affirms omnipotence.
The key is that logical impossibility does not limit power; rather, the question was poorly framed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقال : سوفسطائي . فسأل عن الله أنه أليس على كل شيء قدير ؟ فأجاب بنعم . يقدر على إدخال الدنيا فى بيضة ، فأجاب بالتناقض ؛ لما في ذلك جعل البيضة أوسع منها ، وقد جعلها أضيق منها ؛ إذ هي جزء منها ، وكذلك هذا التأويل في الأصغر والأكبر عن قال الفقيه رحمه الله : وجوابه عندنا أنه أراد بما قال على إبقاء البيضة بغضا للدنيا فهو محال ؛ لما فيها انقلاب بعض كلاً، وكل بعضاً بلا تغير حاله ، وذلك تناقض . وإن أراد بالبيضة وغير البيضة من الدنيا يجعل فيها فهو على وجهين : أحدهما أن يكونا بحالها ، فقد أحال لما ذكر محمد بن شبيب وإن أراد به بذلك تصغير ما قال أو توسيع البيض حتى بسع فيه ما وصف فهو على ذلك قادر ، وبالله التوفيق .
He said: A Sophist asked about God: 'Is He not all-powerful?' He answered 'Yes.' 'He is able to put the world into an egg.' He answered with a contradiction, because in that there is making the egg wider than it, while He made it narrower than it, since it is a part of it. And likewise this interpretation applies to the smaller and the larger. The faqih, may Allah have mercy on him, said: His answer with us is that he intended by what he said, while preserving the egg, to belittle the world, so it is impossible, because in it there is a turning of a part into a whole, and a whole into a part without change in its state, and that is a contradiction. And if he intended by the egg and other than the egg of the world to be placed in it, then it is on two aspects: one of them is that they remain as they are, so it is impossible due to what Muhammad ibn Shabib mentioned. And if he intended by that to make what he said smaller or to expand the egg until it contains what he described, then He is able to do that. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 730

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from a Sophist about God's power. The Sophist poses a paradox: if God is all-powerful, can He put the entire world into an egg? The initial response points out the logical contradiction—making the egg larger than itself while also smaller, since the world contains the egg. The faqih then gives a nuanced answer. He interprets the question in two ways: first, if the intention is to belittle the world while keeping the egg intact, this is impossible because it would require parts and wholes to swap without change—a clear contradiction. Second, if the intention is to actually place the world into the egg, there are two sub-cases: either both remain in their original sizes (impossible), or the egg is expanded to accommodate the world—which God can indeed do. Thus, the apparent paradox is resolved by clarifying the intention, and God's omnipotence is defended.

Key Terms
سوفسطائي
بيضة
تناقض
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the question shows a genuine limit on God's power.
Correction: The question contains a logical flaw; God's power is not limited by impossible scenarios created by ambiguous language.

Checkpoint
According to the faqih, which interpretation of the Sophist's question makes God's action possible?
A) Preserving the egg while belittling the world
B) Keeping both the egg and the world in their current sizes
C) Expanding the egg until it contains the world
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
God's omnipotence is not contradicted by logical impossibilities; such questions reveal misunderstandings about the nature of the request.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a question about Allah's power that seems to create a contradiction, do you immediately feel doubt, or do you pause to examine the assumptions behind the question? This passage teaches us to clarify intentions before concluding. Will you commit to doing this in your own reflection?
?
The Sophist's question was meant to trap, but the faqih responded with careful reasoning. Have you ever been trapped by a doubt because you accepted a question without analyzing it? How can you train yourself to respond with precision rather than react emotionally?
?
This lesson shows that even a seemingly absurd scenario about God's attributes has a logical resolution. What does this tell you about the nature of true knowledge (ʿilm) in Islam? Are you willing to seek deeper understanding rather than quick answers, even when the topic seems difficult or trivial?

Refuting Change in God's Attribute of Creation
Maturidi refutes the claim that God became a creator after not being one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects the Mu'tazilite claim that God was not a creator and then became a creator, and how this preserves the eternity of God's power.

Core Idea
God's power is eternal and unchanging; He was always creator.

Maturidi begins by confronting a critical Mu'tazilite error: the idea that God's attribute of creation could emerge after not existing. This short passage shows why such a notion forces God under a power that changes, which is impossible for the Eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once and then the translation. Notice the compact argument structure.
The phrase 'في حسنير' is unclear, but rely on the overall argument.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The Mu'tazila say God was not a creator then became a creator. Locate where this is stated.
3
Understand Maturidi's objection
Maturidi argues that this claim implies God entered under a power by which He became creator, which would make His power changeable. Ask yourself why that is impossible for God.
This connects to the core idea of God's power being eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم على قولهم : كان غير خالق فصار خالقا ، فقد أدخله من هذا الوجه تحت القدرة التي بها صار خالقا ، فكيف ينكر جواز من لم يكن كذلك ، في حسنير كذلك بأنه خلقة كذلك ، كما صار هو كذلك بأن خلق غيرا ، والله المستعان
تم سئل عن الله : أكان قادرا على أنه خلق الأشياء قبل خلقها ؟ ، زعم نعم ، دليله أن العاجز ممنوع ، فدل وجود المحدث على قدرته ، وإذا كان هو قادرا بذاته . لا بما يعرض من القدرة ، فهو موصوف بالقدرة على الدنيا وأمثالها مما لا يحصى .
زعمتم أنه يقدر :
قال الفقيه رحمه الله : فيقال له : إذ هو قادر بنفسه لا بقدرة ، يعرض يعرض كيف يقدر على خلق جميع حركات العباد وسكونهم إلى أن يقدرهم عليها فإذا أقدرهم عليها زالت قدرته عليها ، إلا أن يأخذ القية عنهم ، فهاذا وصف القدرة بالذات أو بالعوارض، ومن ذلك وصفه فالقول له بقوة لم يظهر الفعل محال ، وما يحتمل زوال قدرته ، فالقول بالقدرة بذاته على مذهبهم مال وإنما أردت بما ذكرت من أقاويل المعتزلة وإن لم يكن لى إلى ذكرها ليعلم المتأمل أن لا سبيل إلى إثبات التوحيد ودفع معارضات الملحدة على مذهبهم، وأن الحمق من القول في التوحيد قول غيرهم ، ولا قوة إلا بالله تم زنم أن كل / قادر اسبقت قدرته فعله فهو وصف من قدر بغير وفعله بغيره ، فهو يتحول من خال إلى حال ، وتقبل ذاته الاستحالة والزوال فأما الله سبحانه فبنفسه يقدر على الأشياء ويفعلها ، فما يذكره في ذلك فاست ولا قوة إلا بالله
حاجة عالمة
(1
وقد بينا فيما تقدم بأبلغ من هذا ، وبالله التوفيق ، وفي هذا آية جعل ذاته ٢ ، وقد بينا وهمه
Then according to their saying: He was not a creator then became a creator, so He entered from this aspect under the power by which He became a creator, so how does he deny the permissibility of one who was not like that, so [؟] likewise because He created [؟] likewise, as He became likewise by creating another. And Allah is the Helper.
Then it was asked about Allah: Was He able to create things before He created them? He claimed yes, his proof is that the incapable is prevented, so the existence of the originated indicates His power. And since He is powerful by His essence (bi-dhatihi), not by an accident of power, He is described as having power over the world and its like beyond count.
You claim that He is able:
The jurist (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It is said to him: Since He is powerful by Himself, not by a power, [؟] how is He able to create all the movements of servants and their stillness, until He gives them power over them, and when He gives them power over them, His power over them ceases, unless He takes the due from them? This is a description of power by essence or by accidents, and from that description, saying He has a power that does not appear in action is impossible, and what admits of His power ceasing, so saying He is powerful by essence on their doctrine is [؟]. I only intended by what I mentioned of the sayings of the Mu'tazila—though I had no need to mention them—so that the contemplator knows that there is no way to establish tawhid and repel the objections of the heretics on their doctrine, and that foolishness in speech about tawhid is the speech of others. And there is no power except with Allah. Then you claim that every powerful one whose power precedes his action, he is described as one who has power by something else and his action is by something else, so he changes from one state to another, and his essence admits of change and cessation. As for Allah, exalted, He by Himself is powerful over things and does them. So what he mentions in that is [؟]. And there is no power except with Allah.
A need of knowledge
(1
And we have explained in what preceded more clearly than this, and with Allah is success, and in this is a sign of making His essence [؟], and we have shown his error.
Source pages p. 732

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of several Mu'tazilite positions regarding God's power and creation. Maturidi first addresses the claim that God was not a creator then became a creator, arguing that this would imply God entered under power (qudra) by which He became a creator, which is impossible. He then discusses a question: Was God able to create things before He created them? The answer is yes, because the existence of originated things proves His power, and His power is by His essence, not by an accident. Then a jurist (likely Maturidi himself) responds to a Mu'tazilite claim about God's power over human actions: if God is powerful by Himself, how can He be able to create all human movements and then give power to humans, which would remove His power over those actions? This would either describe power by essence or by accidents, and either leads to absurdities. Maturidi then criticizes the Mu'tazilite position for making tawhid impossible and defends his own doctrine that God's power is eternal and unchanging. He concludes by noting that he has already explained this more clearly elsewhere, and that the Mu'tazilite view is erroneous.

Key Terms
خلق (khalq)
قدرة (qudra)
خالق (khaliq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's attribute of creation could begin after not existing.
Correction: Maturidi shows that would make God subject to change through power, contradicting His eternal nature.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi identify as the problem with the Mu'tazilite claim that God was not a creator then became a creator?
A) It implies God's power can change.
B) It denies God's mercy.
C) It suggests God was weak before creation.
D) It contradicts the Quran.

Key Takeaway
God's power is eternal and unchanging; He was always the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live with the awareness that your very existence is not a result of a decision God made in time, but rather flows from His eternal power? How does this truth change the way you view your dependence on Him every moment?
?
When you face trials, is there a part of you that wishes God would 'change' His plan for you? Based on this lesson, what is the danger of expecting God to alter His eternal attributes?
?
If you often feel that God is distant or that He 'became' forgiving after something you did, how must you correct that thought using the truth that His attributes are unchanging? How can you rest in the permanence of His mercy?

God's Power Over Human Actions and Giving Power
Maturidi discusses God's power over human actions and refutes Mu'tazilite claims about power ceasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's power over human actions does not cease when He gives power to humans, because His power is by essence, not by accident.

Core Idea
God's power by essence never ceases, even when He gives power to humans.

After addressing God's power in creation, Maturidi turns to a more specific Mu'tazilite objection: If God gives power to humans for their actions, does His own power over those actions cease? This passage explains why such a conclusion only follows if power were an accident, not if it is by essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite question and Maturidi's response
Read the Arabic from 'تم سئل عن الله' to 'آية جعل ذاته ٢'. Follow the argument that God is powerful by His essence.
The text contains unclear phrases; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the key distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between power by essence (bi-dhatihi) and power by accident. Note how this distinction resolves the problem of God's power over human actions.
3
Recognize the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that on the Mu'tazilite doctrine, tawhid cannot be properly established. Understand why he says their view is 'foolishness'.

?

?

Where in your life do you make excuses by saying 'I couldn't help it' or 'It was out of my hands'? Does this lesson challenge your view of personal accountability before God?
?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on God's Power Not Ceasing
Maturidi refutes the first Mu'tazila claim about power ceasing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila claim that God's power over actions ceases when He empowers creatures is self-defeating and implies accidental attributes.

Core Idea
God's power is essential, not transferable or removable.

The Mu'tazila argued that if God is powerful by essence, then when He grants power to creatures, His own power over those actions must cease — otherwise, He would be redundant. Maturidi shows that this reasoning forces the conclusion that God's power is an accidental attribute, which contradicts the very principle of tawhid. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to how the Mu'tazila claim is presented and how Maturidi responds.
The passage contains a logical chain: claim → implication → refutation.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim and Maturidi's response
Locate where the Mu'tazila say that God's power over actions ceases after empowering creatures. Then find Maturidi's argument that this would describe power as an accident.
Notice the phrase 'هذا وصف القدرة بالذات أو بالعوارض' – this is the key turning point.
3
Reflect on the implications for tawhid
Consider why Maturidi says this path cannot establish tawhid. How does making God's power accidental undermine His uniqueness?
This connects to the broader lesson about God's essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فزعمتم أنه يقدر : قال الفقيه رحمه الله : فيقال له : إذ هو قادر بنفسه لا بقدرة يعرض كيف يقدر على خلق جميع حركات العباد وسكونهم إلى أن يقدرهم عليها فإذا أقدرهم عليها زالت قدرته عليها، إلا أن يأخذ القية عنهم، فهاذا وصف القدرة بالذات أو بالعوارض، ومن ذلك وصفه فالقول له بقوة لم يظهر الفعل محال، وما يحتمل زوال قدرته، فالقول بالقدرة بذاته على مذهبهم مال وإنما أردت بما ذكرت من أقاويل المعتزلة وإن لم يكن لى إلى ذكرها ليعلم المتأمل أن لا سبيل إلى إثبات التوحيد ودفع معارضات الملحدة على مذهبهم، وأن الحمق من القول في التوحيد قول غيرهم، ولا قوة إلا بالله ثم زنم أن كل قادر اسبقت قدرته فعله فهو وصف من قدر بغير وفعله بغيره، فهو يتحول من خال إلى حال، وتقبل ذاته الاستحالة والزوال فأما الله سبحانه فبنفسه يقدر على الأشياء ويفعلها، فما يذكره في ذلك فاست ولا قوة إلا بالله
And you claimed that He is able... The jurist said, may God have mercy on him: So it is said to him: Since He is powerful by Himself, not by an accidental power, how can He create all the movements and stillness of servants until He empowers them over them, and when He empowers them over them His power over them ceases, unless He takes back from them? So this is description of power by essence or by accidents. And from that is His description: the statement that He has a power whose act does not appear is absurd; and what admits of the removal of His power, the statement that He is powerful by essence according to their school inclines [to falsity]. And I only intended by what I mentioned of the sayings of the Mu`tazila, even though I have no need to mention them, that the ponderer may know that there is no way to establish tawhid and repel the objections of the atheists according to their school, and that folly in speaking about tawhid is the speech of others. And there is no power except with God. Then they claimed that every powerful one whose power precedes his act is described as powerful by something other [than himself] and his act is by something other [than himself], so he transitions from one state to another, and his essence admits of change and removal. As for God the Exalted, He by Himself is able to do things and does them. So what they mention in that is corrupt. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 732

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu`tazilite claim about divine power. The opponent argues that if God is powerful by essence, He would be able to create the actions of servants only until He empowers them; afterwards His power over those actions would cease unless He takes it back. The author responds that this would make power an accidental attribute, not essential. Furthermore, claiming that God has a power whose act does not appear (i.e., power without an act) is impossible. If His power could cease, then saying He is powerful by essence would be contrary to their own principles. The author explains he mentions these views only to show that the Mu`tazilite path cannot establish tawhid or refute atheists. The second objection claims that if a powerful being’s power precedes his act, it implies his power is not essential. The refutation is that this applies to contingent beings, not to God, who is powerful by Himself. Thus the Mu`tazilite arguments are corrupt.

Key Terms
قدرة
بالذات
بالعوارض
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God empowers creatures, His power over their actions diminishes or is transferred.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's power is essential and not subject to transfer or reduction; the Mu'tazila claim erroneously treats power as an accident.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the Mu'tazila claim that God's power over actions ceases when He empowers the servants invalid?
A) Because God's power is accidental
B) Because it would imply God's power is created
C) Because it describes power as an accident, which is not how God's power is
D) Because the servants do not actually act

Key Takeaway
God's power is an essential attribute; any claim that implies it is accidental or transferable contradicts tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power over every action never ceases, even your smallest movements, does that make you feel watched? What would change if you truly internalized that His power is always directly involved in everything you do?
?
When you succeed, do you ever feel that 'your' power was sufficient? How does this lesson's insistence that God's power is essential and never transferred challenge that feeling of self-sufficiency? What can you do today to acknowledge His continuous power in a specific moment?
?
If the Mu'tazila view is wrong and God's power never 'steps back' from your actions, then what does that mean for your own will? Does it make you less free, or more accountable? How can you live in light of that truth this week?

Refuting the Mu'tazila on Power Preceding Act
Maturidi refutes the second Mu'tazila claim about power preceding act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila argument that power preceding act implies non-essential power applies only to contingent beings, not to God.

Core Idea
For God, power and act are simultaneous and essential.

The Mu'tazila also argued that if a being's power precedes its act, then the power must be distinct from the essence, implying the being is powerful by something other than itself. Maturidi refutes this by distinguishing between contingent beings and God, who is powerful by His own essence. This lesson examines that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the structure: first the Mu'tazila claim, then Maturidi's distinction.
The passage is short but dense; pay attention to the contrast between 'كل قادر' and 'الله سبحانه'.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila claim and Maturidi's distinction
Find where the Mu'tazila say that if power precedes act, it implies power is not essential. Then see how Maturidi applies this only to creatures, not God.
Key phrase: 'فأما الله سبحانه فبنفسه يقدر على الأشياء ويفعلها'.
3
Reflect on why this distinction preserves tawhid
Consider that for God, there is no temporal gap between power and act; He is eternally powerful and acts without change. This maintains His transcendence.
This connects to the lesson's learning objective.

So this is description of power by essence or by accidents. And from that is His description: the statement that He has a power whose act does not appear is absurd; and what admits of the removal of His power, the statement that He is powerful by essence according to their school inclines [to falsity]. And I only intended by what I mentioned of the sayings of the Mu`tazila, even though I have no need to mention them, that the ponderer may know that there is no way to establish tawhid and repel the objections of the atheists according to their school, and that folly in speaking about tawhid is the speech of others. And there is no power except with God. Then they claimed that every powerful one whose power precedes his act is described as powerful by something other [than himself] and his act is by something other [than himself], so he transitions from one state to another, and his essence admits of change and removal. As for God the Exalted, He by Himself is able to do things and does them. So what they mention in that is corrupt. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 732

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu`tazilite claim about divine power. The opponent argues that if God is powerful by essence, He would be able to create the actions of servants only until He empowers them; afterwards His power over those actions would cease unless He takes it back. The author responds that this would make power an accidental attribute, not essential. Furthermore, claiming that God has a power whose act does not appear (i.e., power without an act) is impossible. If His power could cease, then saying He is powerful by essence would be contrary to their own principles. The author explains he mentions these views only to show that the Mu`tazilite path cannot establish tawhid or refute atheists. The second objection claims that if a powerful being’s power precedes his act, it implies his power is not essential. The refutation is that this applies to contingent beings, not to God, who is powerful by Himself. Thus the Mu`tazilite arguments are corrupt.

Key Terms
قدرة
بالذات
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God's power is eternal and His acts are temporal, then His power is separate from His essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that for God, power and act are both from His essence; there is no separation or change in His essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the Mu'tazila argument about power preceding act fail for God?
A) Because it applies only to creatures, not to God
B) Because God's power is created
C) Because the Mu'tazila are always wrong
D) Because the passage is too short to contain a valid argument

Key Takeaway
God's power is not temporally prior to His acts; He is powerful and acts by His own essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you instinctively believe that God's power to help you is something He has to 'activate' or is it always present? How would your reliance on Him change if you truly internalized that His power is simultaneous with His will?
?
This lesson refutes the idea that God's power can be separate or prior to His acts. In your own life, have you ever waited for God's power to 'arrive' before acting in obedience? What would it mean to trust that His power is always present in the very moment you need to act?
?
The Mu'tazila thought they found a flaw in God's perfection. Are there areas in your understanding of God where you impose human limitations, like assuming He 'transitions' or 'changes'? What step can you take today to purify your concept of Allah from such false comparisons?

Refuting the Benefit Argument: Divine Grace in Creation
Maturidi refutes the benefit argument and establishes divine grace as evident in creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the claim that God created for benefit and the principle that divine grace is inherently manifest in all creation.

Core Idea
Questioning why God creates equates act with essence and is intellectually invalid.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi confronts a common objection: that God's creative act must have a purpose of benefit. He demonstrates that such reasoning deviates from the proper theological method, which is rational not scriptural. He then articulates a core Maturidi principle: every creation bears evident signs of God's grace, generosity, wisdom, and power. Finally, he explains that questioning God's creative action is tantamount to questioning His essential attributes, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Identify the refutation structure
Notice how Maturidi first criticizes the benefit argument as a deviation and compares it to the Qadariyya's error. This sets the stage for his positive principle.
2
Understand the Maturidi principle
Focus on the sentence 'والأصل عندنا أن الله تعالى لم يخلق خلقا إلا وأثر نعمه عليه ظاهر' - this is the key doctrinal statement. Reflect on what it implies about creation's purpose.
Consider how this principle refutes the need for a separate 'benefit' motive.
3
Grasp the essence-argument
See how Maturidi argues that since God is essentially generous, powerful, and knowing, His creation is a natural expression of those attributes; hence questioning it is like questioning His lordship.
This argument shows the futility of the original question.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله خلق الخلق لنفع الخلق ونفعه ، ما ذكر فانه حيد عن الجواب ؛ لأنه سئل عن خلق الأشياء ، ومن ذكر فهم صنف من الجملة ؛ فلذلك أوجب ذلك حيده وعلى ذلك شأن القدرية فيما يسألون عن خلق / الأفعال فيرجعون في الجواب . إلى فعل الكفر والمعاصى ، وذلك فاسد ؛ لأن طريق هذا سمعي ، والأول الذي وصف عقلي قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأصل عندنا أن الله تعالى لم يخلق خلقا إلا وأثر نعمه عليه ظاهر ، وأدلة جوده فيه بين ، وأنه حكمته بما فيه من دلالة وحدانية موجدها و برهان سلطانه ونفاذ مشيئة فيه محقق ، وعلامة قدرته وعلمه بحقائق الأشياء غير خفى في ذلك الم ولم كانت الحكمة ؟ والسؤال على أنك لم أنعمت ؟ أو لماذا أظهرت جودك ؟ أنت حجة وحدانيتك ؟ إلى آخر ما ذكر محال فاسد لا يقبله عقل ولا يحتمله وسع لقيحه ؛ لذلك بطل هذا النوع من السؤال ، وبالله التوفيق . ثم جائز أن يقال : إذ هو بذاته جواد ، وبذاته قادر ، وبذاته عالم فجاد تخلقه على خلقه ، إذ هو خلقه إذ في قادر على قادر على أن يجود ويظهر مواهبه . وأصل هذا السؤال عندنا فاسد ؛ لأنه يجعل الفعل هو الذات ، وهو به موصوف في الأزل، والسؤال عن ذلك كالسؤال على أنه لم كان رباً عالما ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And his saying that He created creation for the benefit of creation and its benefit—what he mentioned is a deviation from the answer; because he was asked about the creation of things, and what he mentioned is only a part of the whole; therefore that necessitated his deviation. And similarly is the case of the Qadariyya when they are asked about the creation of actions, they return in the answer to the act of disbelief and disobedience, and that is corrupt; because the way of this is scriptural, while the first which he described is rational. The Shaykh (may Allah have mercy on him) said: And the principle with us is that Allah Almighty did not create any creation except that the effect of His grace upon it is apparent, and the proofs of His generosity in it are clear, and that His wisdom is in what it contains of evidence of the oneness of its Originator and proof of His sovereignty and the effectiveness of His will, and a sign of His power and knowledge of the realities of things is not hidden in that. So why is there wisdom? And the question: Why did You not bestow favors? Or why did You manifest Your generosity? You are the proof of Your oneness? And so on—all that is impossible and corrupt, not accepted by intellect nor can its capacity bear it; therefore this type of question is invalid. And success is with Allah. Then it is permissible to say: Since He is generous by His essence, and powerful by His essence, and knowing by His essence, so He generously creates His creation, since He is powerful over being generous and manifesting His gifts. And the basis of this question with us is corrupt; because it makes the act identical to the essence, and He is described by it in pre-eternity. And the question about that is like asking why He is Lord and Knowing. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 733

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that God created for benefit, showing it deviates from the original question. It compares this to the Qadariyya's error in attributing actions to themselves. Then the author states the Maturidi principle: every creation manifests God's grace, generosity, wisdom, unity, power, and knowledge. Questioning why God did not bestow favors or show generosity is absurd and rejected by intellect. Finally, it adds that since God is essentially generous, powerful, and knowing, His creation is an expression of those attributes, and questioning this is like questioning His lordship and knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the question 'why did God create?' is a valid and meaningful inquiry about God's motives.
Correction: This passage shows that such questioning equates act with essence and is intellectually invalid; creation is a manifestation of God's essential attributes, not a contingent act for a purpose.

Checkpoint
What is the Maturidi principle stated in this passage?
A) God created for benefit
B) Every creation shows evidence of God's grace and attributes
C) Creation is unrelated to God's essence
D) God's creation has no purpose

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position is that creation inherently manifests divine attributes, so questioning why God creates is both futile and intellectually unfounded.
