God's Power to Create from Nothing and the Limits of Human Wisdom
Maturidi argues that God's inability to create from nothing would imply need, and reason affirms creation ex nihilo and divine wisdom beyond human grasp.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's ability to create from nothing is a sign of His power and that human intellects may not fully grasp the wisdom behind His actions.

Core Idea
God can create from nothing; human wisdom cannot fully comprehend His actions.

Maturidi continues by refuting the idea that God cannot create something from nothing, which would imply need and weakness. He then asserts that reason itself necessitates accepting the possibility of creation ex nihilo and that God's actions are wise, even if we cannot fully understand that wisdom. This lesson explores these profound conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Look for the phrase 'بطل وصفه بأن لا يقدر على فعل شيء ابتداء لا عن شيء' - it is invalid to describe Him as unable to do something initially from nothing.
2
Understand the implication of denying creation from nothing
Maturidi says that inability to create from nothing would indicate need and weakness. Note how this connects to the earlier negation of need.
3
Grasp the role of reason
The passage states that reason necessitates acknowledging that the world can come from nothing and that God's actions are wise, even if the wise cannot grasp the wisdom. Ask yourself: why is this a necessary conclusion?
4
Final conclusion
The passage ends with the affirmation of God's absolute sovereignty: 'To Him belong creation and command.' Recognize how this ties the entire argument together.

?

Context and Divine Justice in Maturidi's Thought
Maturidi classifies moral categories and applies context-dependence to divine actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle that moral categories (justice/wisdom, injustice/folly) are context-dependent, and that God's actions are always wisely just despite appearances.

Core Idea
Context determines whether an act is just or unjust, wise or foolish.

Maturidi presents a nuanced moral framework: while justice and wisdom are generally good, and injustice and folly generally evil, the same action can shift categories depending on circumstance. He applies this to divine actions, arguing that God's perfect attributes ensure His acts are always wise and just, even if humans cannot perceive the wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a general principle, a crucial exception, examples, and application to God.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the general principle and the exception
The first sentence states that injustice/folly are evil and justice/wisdom are good 'in general' (fi l-jumla). The next phrase introduces the exception: 'but one and the same thing may be wisdom in one situation, folly in another...'
The word 'في الجملة' is key – it means generally, not absolutely.
3
Understand the examples
Maturidi gives examples like drinking medicine (wise when ill, foolish when healthy) and destroying/preserving objects. Reflect on how context changes the moral classification.
These examples ground the abstract principle in daily life.
4
Apply to God
Maturidi argues that God's actions are always at least wisdom and justice because He is generous, self-sufficient, and knowing. Injustice and folly cannot apply to Him because their causes (ignorance and need) are absent in God.
Even if we don't see the wisdom, it doesn't mean it's not there.
5
Reflect on hidden wisdom
The passage notes that the wisdom behind an action may be hidden from a contemplative observer, or he may wish to know it through sense perception. Neither determines the true nature – knowledge is from Allah.
This guards against judging God's actions by incomplete human understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الجور والسفه قبيحان ، وأن العدل والحكمة حسنان في الجملة ، لكن شيئا واحدا قد يكون حكمة في حال ، سفها فى حال ، جورا في حال ، عدلا في حال ، نحو ما ذكرت من شرب الأدوية ، ثم أكل الأشياء وشربها ، ثم إتلاف الأشياء وإبقاؤها من أنواع الجواهر ما للحاجات أو للمجازات أو الحتموق أو لنحو ذلك ، وإذ ثبت حسن الحكمة فى الجملة والعدل ، وقبح السفه والجور ، ولزم وصف الله تعالى فى كل فعل خلقه فى أقل ما يوصف أنه حكمة وعدل أو فضل وإحسان من حيث ثبت أنه جواد كريم غنى عليم ، وبطل أن يلحقه وصف الجور والسفه ؛ لما كان سببها الجهل والحاجة ، قد ثبت انقسام الشيء الواحد على الجور والعدل وعلى الحكمة والسفه سببها الجهل ، وجائز خفى وجه ذلك على الناظر المتأمل ، أو هو بالحس يريد الاطلاع على العلم به ، وقد ثبت احتمال الوجهين ، لا يقع على واحد منهما الحس ، وعلم المتأمل ذلك
Then the principle is that injustice and folly are evil, and that justice and wisdom are good in general, but one and the same thing may be wisdom in one situation, folly in another, injustice in one, justice in another, such as what I mentioned of drinking medicines, then eating and drinking things, then destroying and preserving things from types of substances, whether for needs, or for recompense, or for [al-ḥatmuq?] or the like. And when the goodness of wisdom and justice in general is established, and the evil of folly and injustice, and it is necessary to describe God most high in every action He creates as at least being wisdom and justice or bounty and beneficence, since it is established that He is generous, beneficent, self-sufficient, knowing, and it is invalid that the description of injustice and folly should attach to Him, because their cause is ignorance and need, and it has been established that one thing can be divided between injustice and justice and between wisdom and folly—whose cause is ignorance—and it is possible that its aspect is hidden from the contemplative observer, or he wishes by sense to gain knowledge of it, and both possibilities have been established, but sense does not determine either one, and the knower knows that.
Source pages p. 816

Commentary
This passage classifies moral categories. Maturidi states the general principle: injustice and folly are evil (qabīḥ), while justice and wisdom are good (ḥasan). However, he introduces a crucial nuance: one and the same action can be classified differently depending on context. For example, drinking medicine is wise when ill, but foolish when healthy; destroying property may be just as punishment but unjust as vandalism. He then applies this to God: since God is generous, self-sufficient, and knowing, every one of His actions is at least wise and just, extending to bounty (فضل) and beneficence (إحسان). Injustice and folly cannot apply to God because their causes—ignorance and need—are absent in Him. Even if the wisdom behind an action is hidden from a human observer, that does not imply folly; the action may have a wisdom beyond human comprehension. This guards against the misconception that God's actions must appear wise by human standards.

Key Terms
جور
سفه
عدل
حكمة
جهل
حاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An action's moral classification (wise/foolish, just/unjust) is fixed and inherent to the action itself.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the same action can be wise or foolish, just or unjust, depending on context and circumstances. For God, all actions are wise and just because He lacks ignorance and need.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the cause of injustice and folly?
A) They are caused by ignorance and need.
B) They are caused by deliberate evil.
C) They are caused by lack of power.
D) They are inherent in certain actions.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that moral categories are not fixed but context-dependent, and God's actions are always wise and just, even if their wisdom is hidden from human perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you dismissed a difficult situation as 'unfair' because you could not see the wisdom in it? How does Maturidi's teaching challenge you to revisit that judgment today?
?
The passage says injustice and folly stem from ignorance and need. In what area of your life might you be acting out of ignorance or need rather than wisdom? Will you seek knowledge before acting?
?
If you truly believed that every action of God—including trials you face—is wise and just even when hidden, what would change about how you respond to hardship? Write one concrete commitment you will make this week.

Human Needs and God's Self-Sufficiency
Maturidi contrasts human limitations with God's freedom from need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that human striving involves movement and rest, but God, being self-sufficient and powerful, cannot be subject to need or inability.

Core Idea
God’s self-sufficiency frees Him from human-like needs and limitations.

Maturidi begins by describing the human condition: we achieve goals through movement and rest, and repel fatigue through stillness. He then contrasts this with God, whose established self-sufficiency and power mean He cannot be afflicted by need or aspiration. This foundational distinction sets the stage for understanding divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on human striving
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the description of human limitations: movement and rest are necessary for achieving goals and repelling fatigue.
Note the contrast that is about to come.
2
Contrast with God's attributes
Identify how Maturidi contrasts human needs with God's self-sufficiency and power. God cannot be subject to need or aspiration.
He uses 'فأما' to shift the discussion.
3
Grasp the implication for God's power
Understand that describing God as unable to create from nothing would imply need, which is invalid for God. This is a key argument for divine transcendence.
The word 'بطل' indicates invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى ما يبلغه الهمة والرغبة؛ إذ لا سبيل له إلى مقصوده إلا بالتحرك والزوال، ولا إلى دفع الإعياء والتعب إلا بالقرار والسكون. فأما الله سبحانه إذ ثبت غناه وقدرته بطل أن يعتريه حاجة أو يعتريه همة. وعلى ذلك لما ثبتت قدرته وسلطانه وعلمه بطل وصفه بأن لا يقدر على فعل شيء ابتداء لا عن شيء؛ إذ ذلك علم الحاجة وآية الضعف. وحاجة جميع ما يحس ويبلغه علم البشر هي الدلالة على تقدير العالم، وعالم به قدير غنى، لم يجز إزالة ذلك بالذي عرف غناه وقدرته وحكمته وعلمه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. فلذلك لزم القول بضرورة العقل لجواز كون العالم لا عن شيء، وخروج فعله على الحكمة، وإن عجزت عقول حكماء العالم عن إدراكها لخروج وجه الحكمة عن نهاية قوة عقولهم، على ما بينا من كون الشيء لا عن شيء، ومن جواز فعل ممن لا ينتفع به، وبذلك تظهر حقيقة الأمر له، وأن له الخلق والأمر، ولكل ذى ملك أن يفعل في ملكه على قدر ما ملك منه ما شاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله
To the extent that aspiration and desire reach; for he has no way to his goal except through movement and transition, and no way to repel weariness and fatigue except through rest and stillness. As for Allah the Exalted, when His self-sufficiency and power are established, it is invalid that need afflicts Him or that aspiration afflicts Him. And accordingly, when His power and authority and knowledge are established, describing Him as unable to do anything initially from nothing is invalid; for that is the sign of need and the mark of weakness. And the need of everything that senses and reaches the knowledge of humans is the indication of the estimation of the world, and the world with Him is powerful, self-sufficient; it is not permissible to remove that by what is known of His self-sufficiency, power, wisdom, and knowledge, and there is no power except with Allah. Therefore, it is necessary to assert by rational necessity the permissibility of the world's existence from nothing, and the emergence of His action upon wisdom, even if the intellects of the wise of the world are incapable of apprehending it because the aspect of wisdom exceeds the limit of their intellectual strength, as we have explained about the existence of a thing from nothing and about the permissibility of an act from one who does not benefit from it. And by that, the reality of the matter for Him becomes apparent, and that to Him belong creation and command, and for every possessor of dominion to act in his dominion according to what he possesses as he wills, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 816

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument establishing God's transcendence over human limitations. Maturidi begins by describing human striving: a person achieves goals through movement and rest, and repels fatigue through stillness. But God, once His self-sufficiency (ghina) and power (qudra) are affirmed, cannot be subject to need or aspiration. Since His power, authority, and knowledge are established, it invalidates describing Him as unable to create anything initially from nothing—such inability would indicate need and weakness. The needs of all sensory and cognitive beings point to the world's design, and the world with God is powerful and self-sufficient. Therefore, reason necessarily affirms that the world can come from nothing, and that God's actions are based on wisdom, even if human intellects cannot fully grasp that wisdom. This shows that God alone has creation and command, and every sovereign can act in his domain as he wills.

Key Terms

Correction: Maturidi argues that such inability would actually be a sign of need and weakness, which are impossible for God. God's power is absolute and not subject to human-like constraints.

Creation from Nothing and Divine Wisdom
Maturidi argues for creation ex nihilo and the limits of human wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason necessarily affirms that the world is created from nothing and that God's actions are based on wisdom beyond human comprehension.

Core Idea
Rational necessity demands that the world come from nothing, and God's wisdom surpasses human intellect.

Building on God's attributes, Maturidi argues that the needs of all creatures point to a self-sufficient Creator. Reason compels us to accept creation from nothing, even if we cannot fully grasp the wisdom behind it. This culminates in the recognition of God's absolute sovereignty over creation and command.

Guided Path
1
Understand the indication from need
Read how the need in all perceived things points to a wise Designer. The world's design is evidence of a powerful, self-sufficient Creator.
Maturidi says 'وحاجة جميع ما يحس ... هي الدلالة على تقدير العالم'
2
Grasp rational necessity for creation from nothing
Notice that reason itself requires that the world can come from nothing, and that God's actions are based on wisdom, even if we cannot fully understand it.
The phrase 'لزم القول بضرورة العقل' indicates necessity.
3
Recognize God's absolute sovereignty
Understand the conclusion: God alone has creation and command, and every sovereign acts in his dominion as he wills.
This establishes God's ultimate authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى ما يبلغه الهمة والرغبة؛ إذ لا سبيل له إلى مقصوده إلا بالتحرك والزوال، ولا إلى دفع الإعياء والتعب إلا بالقرار والسكون. فأما الله سبحانه إذ ثبت غناه وقدرته بطل أن يعتريه حاجة أو يعتريه همة. وعلى ذلك لما ثبتت قدرته وسلطانه وعلمه بطل وصفه بأن لا يقدر على فعل شيء ابتداء لا عن شيء؛ إذ ذلك علم الحاجة وآية الضعف. وحاجة جميع ما يحس ويبلغه علم البشر هي الدلالة على تقدير العالم، وعالم به قدير غنى، لم يجز إزالة ذلك بالذي عرف غناه وقدرته وحكمته وعلمه، ولا قوة إلا بالله. فلذلك لزم القول بضرورة العقل لجواز كون العالم لا عن شيء، وخروج فعله على الحكمة، وإن عجزت عقول حكماء العالم عن إدراكها لخروج وجه الحكمة عن نهاية قوة عقولهم، على ما بينا من كون الشيء لا عن شيء، ومن جواز فعل ممن لا ينتفع به، وبذلك تظهر حقيقة الأمر له، وأن له الخلق والأمر، ولكل ذى ملك أن يفعل في ملكه على قدر ما ملك منه ما شاء، ولا قوة إلا بالله
To the extent that aspiration and desire reach; for he has no way to his goal except through movement and transition, and no way to repel weariness and fatigue except through rest and stillness. As for Allah the Exalted, when His self-sufficiency and power are established, it is invalid that need afflicts Him or that aspiration afflicts Him. And accordingly, when His power and authority and knowledge are established, describing Him as unable to do anything initially from nothing is invalid; for that is the sign of need and the mark of weakness. And the need of everything that senses and reaches the knowledge of humans is the indication of the estimation of the world, and the world with Him is powerful, self-sufficient; it is not permissible to remove that by what is known of His self-sufficiency, power, wisdom, and knowledge, and there is no power except with Allah. Therefore, it is necessary to assert by rational necessity the permissibility of the world's existence from nothing, and the emergence of His action upon wisdom, even if the intellects of the wise of the world are incapable of apprehending it because the aspect of wisdom exceeds the limit of their intellectual strength, as we have explained about the existence of a thing from nothing and about the permissibility of an act from one who does not benefit from it. And by that, the reality of the matter for Him becomes apparent, and that to Him belong creation and command, and for every possessor of dominion to act in his dominion according to what he possesses as he wills, and there is no power except with Allah.

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument establishing God's transcendence over human limitations. Maturidi begins by describing human striving: a person achieves goals through movement and rest, and repels fatigue through stillness. But God, once His self-sufficiency (ghina) and power (qudra) are affirmed, cannot be subject to need or aspiration. Since His power, authority, and knowledge are established, it invalidates describing Him as unable to create anything initially from nothing—such inability would indicate need and weakness. The needs of all sensory and cognitive beings point to the world's design, and the world with God is powerful and self-sufficient. Therefore, reason necessarily affirms that the world can come from nothing, and that God's actions are based on wisdom, even if human intellects cannot fully grasp that wisdom. This shows that God alone has creation and command, and every sovereign can act in his domain as he wills.

The Relativity of Moral Attributes
Maturidi establishes the relativity of moral attributes and defends God's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single action can be considered just or unjust depending on context, and why this principle leads to the conclusion that all of God's actions are wise and just.

Core Idea
One action may be wisdom in one context and foolishness in another.

Imam Maturidi begins by establishing a general principle: injustice and foolishness are inherently ugly, while justice and wisdom are inherently good. However, he immediately introduces a crucial nuance: the same action can take on different moral qualities depending on the circumstances. In this lesson, we will examine this principle and see how it safeguards God's transcendence from any attribution of injustice or foolishness.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the general moral principle
Maturidi states that injustice and foolishness are ugly, while justice and wisdom are good in general. This sets the framework.
2
Discover the relativity of moral attributes
Note that one and the same thing can be wisdom in one situation and foolishness in another, as in the example of drinking medicine.
3
Apply the principle to God
Since God is bountiful, generous, self-sufficient, and knowing, His actions cannot be unjust or foolish because these stem from ignorance and need, which are absent in God.
4
Understand the refutation of dualism
Because the same action can be just or unjust depending on context, positing two separate principles (good and evil) is invalid; human knowledge is limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل أن الجور والسفه قبيحان، وأن العدل والحكمة حسنان في الجملة، لكن شيئا واحدا قد يكون حكمة في حال، سفها في حال، جورا في حال، عدلا في حال، نحو ما ذكرت من شرب الأدوية، ثم أكل الأشياء وشربها، ثم إتلاف الأشياء وإبقاؤها من أنواع الجواهر ما للحاجات أو للمجازات أو الحتموق أو لنحو ذلك، وإذ ثبت حسن الحكمة في الجملة والعدل، وقبح السفه والجور، ولزم وصف الله تعالى في كل فعل خلقه في أقل ما يوصف أنه حكمة وعدل أو فضل وإحسان من حيث ثبت أنه جواد كريم غنى عليم، وبطل أن يلحقه وصف الجور والسفه؛ لما كان سببها الجهل والحاجة، قد ثبت انقسام الشيء الواحد على الجور والعدل وعلى الحكمة والسفه سببها الجهل، وجائز خفى وجه ذلك على الناظر المتأمل، أو هو بالحس يريد الاطلاع على العلم به، وقد ثبت احتمال الوجهين، لا يقع على واحد منهما الحس، وعلم المتأمل ذلك، بطل قضاؤه في شريعته، على الإشارة إليه بالحكمة والسفه والعدل والجور، فلزم بهذا جهل كل البشر لمعرفة حقيقة الأمرين في الشيء بالتأمل فيه أن يعرف الأسباب التي بها تتغير أحوال المحسوسات على الحواس، وإذا ثبت ذا بطل قول الثنوية بالإثنين
Then the principle is that injustice (al-jawr) and foolishness (al-safah) are ugly (qabihan), and that justice (al-ʿadl) and wisdom (al-ḥikma) are good (hasanan) in general. However, one and the same thing may be wisdom in one situation, foolishness in another, injustice in one situation, justice in another, like what I mentioned of drinking medicines, then eating and drinking things, then destroying and preserving things of various types of substances for needs or retributions or [؟] or the like. And when the goodness of wisdom and justice in general, and the ugliness of foolishness and injustice are established, and it is necessary to describe Allah the Exalted in every act He creates in the least that He is described as wisdom and justice or bounty and beneficence, since it is established that He is bountiful, generous, self-sufficient, knowing, and it becomes invalid that the description of injustice and foolishness attaches to Him, because their cause is ignorance and need. It has been established that the same thing is divided into injustice and justice and into wisdom and foolishness, their cause being ignorance, and it is possible that its aspect is hidden from the careful observer, or he is by sense desiring to attain knowledge of it. And it has been established that it admits of both aspects, sense does not fall upon either one, and the knowledgeable observer knows that, his judgment in His law becomes invalid, upon pointing to it as wisdom and foolishness, justice and injustice. So by this, the ignorance of all humans of knowing the true nature of the two matters in the thing through reflection is necessitated, that he knows the causes by which the states of sensibles change upon the senses. And when this is established, the statement of the dualists (al-thunawiyya) about the two [principles] becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 816

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument about the relativity of moral attributes like justice (ʿadl), wisdom (ḥikma), injustice (jawr), and foolishness (safah). Maturidi begins by stating the general principle: injustice and foolishness are ugly, justice and wisdom are good. However, one and the same action can be wisdom in one situation and foolishness in another, or just in one and unjust in another, as in the example of drinking medicine. Since God is established as bountiful, generous, self-sufficient, and knowing, every act of His must be described as wisdom, justice, bounty, or beneficence. Injustice and foolishness cannot apply to Him because their causes—ignorance and need—are impossible for God. The same act can be divided into justice or injustice based on circumstances, and its aspect may be hidden from the observer. Sense perception does not reveal either aspect; only the knowledgeable observer can know. Because humans are ignorant of the true nature of these matters in objects due to changing circumstances, the claim of the dualists (who posit two ultimate principles, good and evil) is invalidated.

الجور
السفه
العدل
الحكمة
الثنوية
الجهل
الحاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An action is always just or always unjust irrespective of context.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the same action can be just in one context and unjust in another, so moral judgment depends on circumstances.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say the dualists' (al-thunawiyya) argument is invalid?
A) Because they deny God's attributes
B) Because they claim two separate principles when one action can be both good and evil depending on context
C) Because they reject the existence of evil
D) Because they affirm human free will

Key Takeaway
One action can be just in one context and unjust in another; therefore, God's actions, being from the All-Knowing, Self-Sufficient, are always wise and just.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you face a trial or loss that you cannot understand, do you truly trust that Allah's action is wise and just, or do you secretly doubt His wisdom? What would it take for you to submit completely to His judgment even when it appears foolish to your limited mind?

The dualists believed in two separate sources of good and evil. Do you ever act as if there are sources of harm outside Allah's decree—blaming 'bad luck' or 'fate'? Reflect on a specific instance and ask: How can you train yourself to see every event as coming from the All-Wise and Just, even when you cannot see the wisdom?

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite View on Harmful Actions
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that any action without benefit is unwise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that any action without direct benefit is unwise, by showing that all harmful events contain hidden wisdoms.

Core Idea
No harm is without hidden benefit—wisdom lies in every event.

The Mu'tazilites argued that a wise being would never cause harm without immediate benefit. Imam Maturidi counters this by pointing out that even harmful events carry multiple forms of benefit—as signs, lessons, reminders, or warnings. This lesson unpacks his argument and the types of benefit he enumerates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the confident opening statement 'وبطل' (And false is).
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite claim
The passage begins by stating the false view: that any action not benefiting another is not wisdom. This is the target of refutation.
The Mu'tazilite position equates wisdom with direct benefit.
3
List the benefits of harm
Maturidi lists several ways harmful actions can benefit: as a sign (دلالة), admonition (موعظة), reminder of blessings, warning of punishment, and definition of God's sovereignty. Count them.
There are at least five types mentioned.
4
Understand the refutation logic
The argument is that since every harmful event can benefit in at least one of these ways, the original Mu'tazilite premise is false.
This is a direct refutation by counterexample.
5
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider how this teaching applies to your own understanding of life events that seem harmful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل من يقول من المعتزلة أن كل فعل لا ينفع آخر فهو غير حكمة. مع ما لا يوجد ضرر البتة إلا وأمكن أن ينتفع به أحد إما من طريق الدلالة، أو من طريق الموعظة، أو ما فيه من تذكير النعمة وتحذير النقمة، ومن تعريف من له الخلق والأمر في الخلق، وغير ذلك مما يكثر ذكره، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And false is the statement of those from the Mu'tazila who say that every action that does not benefit another is not wisdom. While there is no harm at all except that it is possible for someone to benefit from it, either by way of indication, or by way of admonition, or what is in it of reminding of blessing and warning of punishment, and of defining who has creation and command in creation, and other things too numerous to mention, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific Mu'tazilite claim. The Mu'tazila held that any action that does not benefit another person is not an act of wisdom (ḥikma). Maturidi argues that this is false because there is no harmful action (ḍarar) from which someone cannot benefit. The benefit may come through various means: as a sign (dalāla), as a moral lesson (mawʿiẓa), as a reminder of blessings (tadhkīr al-niʿma), as a warning of punishment (taḥdhīr al-niqma), or as a definition of who holds creation and command. Thus, even seemingly harmful events have hidden wisdom. The passage ends with the formula 'there is no power except with Allah.'

الحكمة
الضرر
النفع
الدلالة
الموعظة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an action causes harm, it cannot be from a wise God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that harm can bring benefits like signs, lessons, reminders, and warnings, so it is still wise.

Checkpoint

Two Causes of Foolishness and Their Negation from Allah
Maturidi classifies foolishness into two causes in the witnessed world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational causes of foolishness in human actions and why they cannot apply to Allah.

Core Idea
Foolishness requires transgression or disobedience – both impossible for Allah.

Before discussing God's attributes, Maturidi establishes a principle from our witnessed world: what makes an action foolish? By identifying the two causes, he then shows why such a description can never be applied to Allah. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: it starts with a principle, lists two causes, then negates them for Allah.
The phrase 'and there is no power except with Allah' is a closing prayer.
2
Identify the two causes of foolishness
Locate the two causes: (1) transgression of dominion without permission, (2) riding a prohibition and opposing command. Understand how each makes an action foolish in human affairs.
The term 'safah' (foolishness) is often contrasted with wisdom (hikmah).
3
Apply the negation to Allah
Maturidi states that both causes are impossible for Allah because He is the ultimate owner of all dominion and has no superior to disobey. Ask yourself: why can't these causes exist for Allah?
This establishes divine transcendence above foolishness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الذي يجعل الفعل في الشاهد سفها أحد أمرين: إما تعدى الملك لا بإذن من له الملك لذلك الفعل، أو لما فيه ركوب نهى، ومخالفة الأمر ممن له الأمر والنهى، وكل ذلك عن الله جل ثناؤه منفى، ثبت أنه يتعالى عن احتمال لحوق هذا الوصف فعله، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the principle that makes an action foolish (safah) in the witnessed world is one of two matters: either transgression of dominion without permission of the one who has dominion over that action, or because it involves riding a prohibition and opposing the command of the one who has command and prohibition. And all of that is negated concerning Allah, may His praise be exalted; it is established that He is exalted above the possibility of this description attaching to His action, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage is a classification of what constitutes foolishness (safah) in human actions. Maturidi states that an action is considered foolish in the witnessed world (al-shāhid) for one of two reasons: (1) transgression of another's dominion without permission, i.e., acting beyond one's authority; (2) violating a prohibition and disobeying the one who has the right to command and forbid. Both of these are impossible for God, because He is the ultimate owner of all dominion and has no superior to disobey. Therefore, God's actions cannot be described as foolish. The passage ends with the phrase 'and there is no power except with Allah.'

Key Terms
السفه
الشاهد
الملك
النهى
الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any apparently harmful or seemingly pointless action by God could be considered foolish.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that foolishness is defined by transgression or disobedience, not by harm or apparent lack of benefit. Since Allah cannot transgress (He owns all) and cannot disobey (no superior), His actions are beyond foolishness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two causes that make an action foolish in this world?
A) Ignorance and need
B) Transgression of dominion without permission and disobedience to command
C) Harm and lack of benefit
D) Weakness and inability

Key Takeaway
An action is only foolish if it involves transgression or disobedience, and since Allah has absolute dominion and no superior, His actions are beyond such description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you ever judge events as 'foolish' because they don't align with your desires? How does knowing that Allah's actions are beyond such categories change the way you process disappointment?
?
Have you ever acted as if you had authority over matters that truly belong to Allah alone? This lesson teaches that foolishness arises from transgressing boundaries. Which area of your life do you need to acknowledge His dominion more deeply?
?
If Allah's actions can never be foolish, then what trial are you currently enduring that you must trust completely in His wisdom, even though you cannot see the reason? What would it cost you to surrender that complaint today?

The Absolute Evil of Lying Compared to Other Actions
Maturidi distinguishes lying from actions that can be wisdom or foolishness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi says lying is never permissible in any situation, unlike other actions that can vary in moral value based on context.

Core Idea
Lying is always evil; other actions' morality depends on context.

Maturidi draws a sharp contrast between lying and actions that can be either wise or foolish, just or unjust. He argues that lying is inherently evil and never becomes good, while other actions receive their moral valuation from their circumstances. This distinction helps clarify how we describe God's actions and why we must be cautious in judging them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the flow of Maturidi's argument.
2
Identify the contrast
Notice how Maturidi contrasts lying with actions that can be divided into wisdom/foolishness and justice/injustice. What is the key difference?
Lying is never suitable in any situation.
3
Apply to God's attributes
Consider why Maturidi uses this distinction to affirm God's transcendence. How does this protect against attributing foolishness or injustice to God?
We cannot judge God's actions based on limited human knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس ذلك كالكذب؛ لأنه لا يصلح بحال، كالفعل الذي ينقسم على الحكمة والسفه والعدل والجور، وهذا من حيث الجملة لا انقلاب له، ومن حيث الوقوع في شيء على الإشارة إليه ممكن فيه الأمران باختلاف الأحوال والأسباب؛ لذلك لزم وصف الله تعالى في الجملة بالتعالى عن فعل السفه والجور وفي الإشارة أيضا. لكن لا يجوز أن يوصف فيما ظهر فعله بالسفه والجور بما لا يبلغه علم البشر ولا يدركه عقل، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And that is not like lying (al-kidhb); because it is never suitable in any situation, unlike the action that is divided into wisdom, foolishness, justice, and injustice. And this [lying] in general has no reversal, but in terms of occurrence in something pointed to, it is possible for both matters [to occur] due to differing circumstances and causes. Therefore, it is necessary to describe Allah the Exalted in general as exalted above foolish and unjust actions, and also in the specific indication. However, it is not permissible to describe what appears as His action as foolishness or injustice by what the knowledge of humans does not reach nor the intellect perceives, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage is a theological distinction between lying (kadhib) and other actions that can vary in moral value. Maturidi explains that lying is never permissible under any circumstances, unlike actions that can be wisdom or foolishness, justice or injustice depending on context. Lying has no reversal (lā inqilāb lahu)—it is always bad. However, when considering a specific action that could be interpreted as either, it may admit both possibilities due to differing circumstances. Therefore, God must be described in general as exalted above foolishness and injustice, and also in specific cases. But we cannot label any apparent action of God as foolish or unjust if human knowledge and intellect cannot grasp its true wisdom. The passage ends with the usual formula.

Key Terms
الكذب
الحكمة
السفه
العدل
الجور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if lying is always wrong, then God cannot lie; but Maturidi uses this to highlight that other actions are context-dependent.
Correction: Maturidi's point is not about God's inability to lie, but that God's actions, unlike lying, cannot be judged by human standards of foolishness or injustice because they may be wise in ways we don't understand.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say lying is different from actions like wisdom or foolishness?
A) Lying can sometimes be good
B) Lying is never appropriate in any situation, while others can vary
C) Lying is a divine attribute
D) Lying is only wrong when it harms someone

Key Takeaway
Lying is absolutely evil and never becomes good, unlike other actions whose morality depends on context; therefore, we cannot judge God's actions based on limited human understanding.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a small lie because you thought the outcome was good? How does Maturidi's absolute condemnation of lying challenge that? What step will you take today to repent and commit to truthfulness, even when it costs you?
?
You often judge others' actions as foolish or unjust. But if even God's actions are beyond your full comprehension, how can you be so certain about the actions of fellow humans? Pause and ask Allah to grant you humility in judgment.
?
If lying is always evil, what does that say about the 'white lies' you tell to avoid discomfort? Search your heart: have you placed your own ease above the command of Allah? What concrete change in your speech will you make starting now?

The Intrinsic Ugliness of Injustice in the Intellect
Maturidi presents the first of two ways to know the corruption of describing God with injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's first way of proving that describing God with injustice, foolishness, or lying is false: these qualities are inherently recognized as ugly by the intellect through both intuition and reflection, and this ugliness only increases with contemplation, unlike conventional ugliness that can become acceptable through habit.

Core Idea
Injustice is intrinsically ugly to the rational mind, not just a matter of habit.

Maturidi begins his discussion of divine attributes by establishing a clear rational foundation. He asserts that certain qualities, like injustice and lying, are inherently recognized as ugly by the intellect, both through immediate intuition and through deeper reflection. This sets them apart from things that are conventionally considered ugly but can become acceptable through repeated exposure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to absorb Maturidi's language. Note that the passage is cut off; only the first way is fully explained.
2
Identify the two aspects mentioned
The passage announces two ways (وجهان) to know the corruption of describing God with injustice, etc. Try to spot where the first way ends and where the second way might begin.
The second way is not included in this excerpt.
3
Understand the first way: intrinsic ugliness
Maturidi says the ugliness of these qualities is known in the intellect by both badiha (intuition) and fikr (reflection). Its ugliness does not decrease but only increases with contemplation.
This is unlike things that are ugly by nature (qabih bi al-tab‛) which can become good through habit.
4
Contrast with conventional ugliness
Consider the example of slaughter (al-dhabh) which is naturally ugly but becomes acceptable through continued practice. Why is injustice different according to Maturidi?
Injustice remains ugly no matter how often it is repeated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Then the totality of what is known by it the corruption of describing [God] with injustice, foolishness, and lying are two aspects: one of them is the ugliness of that in intellects by intuition (al-badiha) and reflection (al-fikr) together, so that it does not increase upon contemplation and investigation except in ugliness, nor upon prolonged consideration except in foulness. And that is not like the naturally ugly (al-qabih bi al-tabʿ); that becomes good by habituation and long use, like slaughter and its types, and likewise we find...
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage is a summary that provides two ways to know the corruption of attributing injustice, foolishness, or lying to God. Maturidi presents the first aspect: these qualities are inherently ugly in the intellect, both through intuition (badiha) and reflection (fikr). Their ugliness only increases upon deeper contemplation, unlike things that are considered ugly by natural disposition (qabīḥ bi al-tabʿ) which can become good through habituation and long practice, like slaughter. The second aspect is not mentioned as the passage is cut off. The text ends mid-sentence, likely continuing on the next page.

Maturidi's Refutation of Dualism and Mu'tazilite Benefit Theory
Maturidi refutes dualism and Mu'tazilite benefit theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects human criteria for judging Allah's actions and how this refutes both dualism and the Mu'tazilite claim that every action must have a discernible benefit.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom transcends human perception; what seems harmful may be beneficial.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses two critical theological errors: the dualist belief in two independent principles (good and evil) and the Mu'tazilite claim that every divine action must have a discernible benefit. Maturidi argues that both errors stem from a failure to recognize that human knowledge of the 'aspects of wisdom' is limited. He then provides examples of how even apparent harm can serve greater purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the two refutations: against dualism and against the Mu'tazilite view.
The passage is dense; reading it twice helps.
2
Analyze the refutation of dualism
Maturidi first argues that human judgment of God's actions as wise or foolish is invalid because humans cannot fully grasp the reality of things. This ignorance invalidates dualism, as dualists misunderstand wisdom in creating harm and benefit.
Key phrase: 'فجهلهم بوجوه الحكمة فى خلق الضار والنافع'.
3
Examine the refutation of the Mu'tazilite view
Maturidi then refutes those who say every action must benefit another. He lists ways even harm can be beneficial: indication, admonition, reminding of blessing, warning of punishment, and defining who has creation and command.
List these ways and reflect on how they show hidden benefits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطل قضاؤه في شريعته ، على الإشارة إليه بالحكمة والسفه والعدل والجور ، فلزم بهذا جهل كل البشر لمعرفة حقيقة الأمرين فى الشيء بالتأمل فيه أن يعرف الأسباب التى بها تتغير أحوال المحسوسات على الحواس ، وإذا ثبت ذا بطل قول الثنوية بالإثنين ، فجهلهم بوجوه الحكمة فى خلق الضار والنافع ، إذ قد يجوز أن يصير كل ضار فى حال نافعا فى أخرى. وبطل من يقول من المعتزلة . أن كل فعل لا ينفع آخر فهو غير حكمة . مع ما لا يوجد ضرر البتة إلا وأمكن أن ينتفع ينتفع به أحد إما من طريق الدلالة ، أو من طريق الموعظة ، أو ما فيه من تذكير النعمة وتحذير النقمة ، ومن تعريف من له الخلق والأمر في الخلق ، وغير ذلك مما يكثر ذكره ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
His judgment in His law is invalid, by being referred to as wisdom and foolishness and justice and injustice. So this necessitates the ignorance of all humans of knowing the reality of the two matters in a thing through reflection on it, to know the causes by which the states of sensed things change upon the senses. And if this is established, the statement of the dualists of two [principles] is invalidated, due to their ignorance of the aspects of wisdom in creating the harmful and the beneficial, for it is possible that every harmful [thing] may become beneficial in another state. And invalidated is the statement of those among the Mu'tazila who say that every action that does not benefit another is unwise, while there is no harm at all except that it is possible for someone to benefit from it, either by way of indication, or by way of admonition, or what is in it of reminding of blessing and warning of punishment, and of defining who has creation and command in creation, and other than that which its mention is abundant. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage is a dual refutation. Maturidi first argues that if God's actions could be labeled as wise or foolish, just or unjust based on human investigation, then all humans would be ignorant of the true reality of these matters in things, because changes in sensory perceptions depend on causes that humans cannot fully grasp. This establishes the invalidity of dualism (the belief in two principles, good and evil) since dualists fail to understand wisdom behind both harmful and beneficial things. He then refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that an action which does not benefit another is unwise, showing that every harm can potentially benefit someone through various means such as indicating a truth, offering a lesson, reminding of blessings, warning of punishment, or defining who has creation and command. Thus, nothing occurs without some benefit, and no power exists except through Allah.

Key Terms
ثنوية (Dualism)
حكمة (Wisdom)
سفه (Foolishness)
عدل (Justice)
جور (Injustice)
محتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi denies any possibility of understanding divine wisdom.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that humans cannot fully grasp divine wisdom, but it is not arbitrary; he shows specific ways harm can be beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the statement of the dualists invalidated?
A) Because they believe in two gods
B) Because they are ignorant of the aspects of wisdom in creating harmful and beneficial things
C) Because they deny the existence of harm
D) Because they claim all actions are beneficial

Key Takeaway
Divine wisdom is beyond human comprehension; what appears harmful may contain hidden benefits that only Allah knows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial that seems purely harmful, do you trust that Allah has hidden wisdom and benefit you cannot yet see? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that every harm carries a purpose from Him?
?
Have you ever withheld obedience because you did not see immediate benefit, thinking it wise to delay? How does Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazilite view expose the arrogance behind that calculation?
?
This lesson declares that humans are ignorant of the true reality of things. Is there an area in your life where you insist on understanding why Allah allowed something before you submit? Will you now confess that you cannot know the full picture and surrender to His wisdom?

The Two Causes of Foolishness and Their Negation from Allah
Maturidi enumerates the two causes of foolishness in human actions and negates them for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational causes of foolishness in human actions and why they cannot apply to Allah's actions, establishing His transcendence.

Core Idea
Foolishness comes from overstepping limits; Allah has no limits to overstep.

Imam Maturidi presents a rational argument that any action considered foolish falls under one of two specific causes: acting without ownership or violating a command. Since Allah is the absolute owner and commander, neither cause can apply to Him. This passage lays the foundation for understanding divine transcendence (tanzih) in a rational framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the structure: Maturidi defines the principle (أصل) first, then applies it to Allah.
Pay attention to the logical flow from definition to negation.
2
Identify the two causes
Find the two distinct causes: تعدى الملك (transgression of ownership) and ركوب نهى (violating a prohibition/command). Understand the wording.
3
Understand the negation
Notice the phrase 'وكل ذلك عن الله منفي' – all that is negated from Allah. Ask why.
The negation is based on the impossibility of the conditions for foolishness in Allah.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
This shows that divine actions are not subject to human moral categories like foolishness because the conditions for such judgments are absent in Allah.
This reinforces Allah's absolute sovereignty and wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأصل الذى يجعل الفعل فى الشاهد سفها أحد أمرين : إما تعدى الملك لا بإذن من له الملك لذلك الفعل ، أو لما فيه ركوب نهى ، ومخالفة الأمر ممن له الأمر والنهى ، وكل ذلك عن الله جل ثناؤه منفى ، ثبت أنه يتعالى عن احتمال لحوق هذا الوصف فعله ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then the principle that makes an action in the witnessed world foolish is one of two matters: either transgression of ownership without permission from the owner of that action, or because it involves riding a prohibition and contradicting the command from the one who has command and prohibition. And all of that is negated concerning Allah, may His praise be exalted. It is established that He is exalted above the possibility of this description attaching to His action. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This passage defines the principle of foolishness in observable human actions. Maturidi states that an action is considered foolish for one of two reasons: (1) it exceeds the limits of one's authority by acting without the permission of the rightful owner, or (2) it violates a prohibition or contradicts a command from the one who has the right to command and prohibit. Since both of these are impossible for God—He is the absolute owner and commander with no one above Him—it follows that His actions cannot be described as foolish. This establishes God's transcendence above any charge of foolishness or injustice.

Key Terms
أصل (Principle)
سفه (Foolishness)
تعدى الملك (Transgression of ownership)
ركوب نهى (Riding a prohibition)
أمر ونهى (Command and prohibition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think that God's actions can be judged as foolish by human reasoning.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that the criteria for foolishness (acting without ownership or violating a command) do not apply to Allah, so His actions transcend such judgments.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two causes that make an action foolish in the witnessed world?
A) Harm and benefit
B) Transgression of ownership and violating a command
C) Ignorance and need
D) Pride and arrogance

Key Takeaway
An action is foolish only if it exceeds authority or disobeys a command; since Allah has no superior, His actions cannot be foolish.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or something that seems unfair in your life, do you ever catch yourself silently accusing Allah of being unwise or unjust? How does this lesson's distinction between human foolishness and divine action challenge that inner accusation? What verse or phrase from this passage can you memorize to combat that thought?
?
Consider a relationship where you have authority over others (e.g., parent, employer, teacher). Have you ever used that authority in a way that was foolish—by overstepping bounds or giving commands without wisdom? How does the two-part definition of foolishness in this passage expose your own faults? What step will you take today to correct that?
?
This lesson says that Allah is exalted above foolishness because He has no superior. But you and I have superiors: parents, teachers, laws, and ultimately Allah. In what area of your life are you currently acting as if you have no superior, and how does that make your actions foolish? What is one command or boundary you have been ignoring, and what will you do about it right now?

The Distinction Between Rational and Natural Repugnance
Maturidi distinguishes rational from natural repugnance and shows the former is immutable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's distinction between rational ugliness (al-qabīḥ bi-l-'aql) and natural ugliness (al-qabīḥ bi-l-ṭab') and why the former is immutable.

Core Idea
Rational ugliness never becomes good, unlike natural repulsiveness that can be tamed by habit.

Maturidi begins by addressing a key question: Are all repugnant actions alike? He distinguishes between actions that are inherently ugly according to reason and those that are merely distasteful by nature. This distinction is crucial for understanding that God cannot be described with truly evil actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two kinds of ugliness
The passage presents two types of ugliness: rational (قبيح بالعقل) and natural (قبيح بالطبع). Notice how each is defined and how they differ in their mutability.
Rational ugliness never becomes acceptable, while natural ugliness can become good through habit.
3
Reflect on the example of wild animals
Maturidi uses the example of wild beasts and birds that become tame through training. This illustrates how natural repugnance can change. Ask yourself: can rational ugliness ever change like this?
4
Note the conclusion
The passage concludes that rational ugliness only increases with scrutiny. This has profound implications for how we describe God.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس ذلك كالكذب ؛ لأنه لا يصلح بحال ، كالفعل الذى الحكمة والسفه والعدل والجور ، وهذا من حيث الجملة لا انقلاب له ، ومن حيث الوقوع في شيء على الإشارة إليه ممكن فيه الأمران باختلاف الأحوال والأسباب ؛ لذلك لزم وصف الله تعالى فى الجملة بالتعالى عن فعل السفه والجور وفى الإشارة أيضا . لكن لا يجوز أن يوصف فيما ظهر فعله بالسفه والجور بما لا يبلغه علم البشر ولا يدركه عقل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم جملة ما يعلم به فساد الوصف بالجور والسفه والكذب وجهان : أحدهما قبح ذلك في العقول بالبديهة والفكر جميعا ، حتى لا يزداد عند التأمل والبحث عنه إلا قبحا ، ولا عند طول النظر فيه إلا فحشا ، وليس ذلك كالقبيح بالطبع ، إن ذلك يصير حسنا بالاعتياد وطول الصحة كالذبح وأنواع ذلك ، وكذلك نجد جواهر الدواب والسباع والطيور مستوحشة عن الناس بالطباع ، نافرة عما يراد بها من أنواع المكاسب والأعمال ، ثم تخرج عنها بالرياضة والتعليم حتى يألف بالذي كان تنفر عنه ، ويصير ذلك له كأنه الطباع المجبول ، ولا يكون الذي قبح بالعقل بهذا الوصف أبدا ، بل يزداد على طول النظر في شأنه . ثم على ذلك من احتمل فعله ذلك لا يوثق لوعده ولا يخاف وعيده ، ولا يرغب في خبره ولا يؤمن شره ، ومن ذا شأنه وعمله فمحال احتمال إضافة مثله إلى العليم الحكيم بذاته الغنى بنفسه ، مع الوصف بأن لا يخفى عليه شيء ، ولا يصعب عليه أمر فيها أراد : ، بل على قول المعتزلة لا يؤمن منه هذا ؛ إذ قد تخرج أكثر الأشياء عن إرادته ، ويوجد ما لا يريده فى سلطانه منه بلا سلطان له في الإخراج عنه ؛ إذ لم يرده ، ويريد زيادة سلطان ، ويتولى ذلك أن يكون ويتولى ذلك أن يكون ، فيمنع عن ذلك ، نحو ما يريد أن يكون جميع خلقه مطيعين ، خلقه مطيعين ، ويكون له فى سلطانه وملكه الطاعة لا المعاصي فلا يكون ، ثم قد كان وعد لقوم مُدَدًا لأعمارهم ، وهو المبقى لهم ، وكان في وعده أن يرزقهم إلى تلك المدد أنواع الرزق ويسوق إليهم أنواع الخيرات ، فيأتى خلق من خلائقه فيقتلوهم قبل مضي المدة ، فيمنعه عن إنجاز ما وعدوا الوفاء بالفعل الذى أخبرهم أن يفعله من إبقاء حياتهم إلى تلك المدة ، وفى ذلك ايجاب الحاجة ولحوق الكذب اللذين يحققان السفه والجور له القدرة على الظلم والجور والسفه والكذب ، وكل فعل لو كان لأسقط الربوبية وأزال الإلهية ، فأدخلو إلهيته وربوبيته تحت القدرة والتدبير ، فمتى يكون حاله سكون القلب بالوفاء بالذى وعد ، ولا قوة إلا بالله ما كان موصوفا بالجود والكرم والعفو والإحسان ، وفى الفعل بالوصف مع الذي ذكرنا زواله جل عن ذلك وتعالى .
And that is not like lying, because it is never suitable, like the action that [involves] wisdom and foolishness and justice and injustice. And this, from a general perspective, has no change, and from the perspective of occurrence in something that can be referred to, the two matters are possible in it due to differing states and causes. Therefore, it is necessary to describe Allah, exalted, in general as being exalted above the action of foolishness and injustice, and also in reference. But it is not permissible to describe His manifest action as foolishness and injustice based on what human knowledge does not reach and intellect does not perceive. And there is no power except through Allah. Then the summary of what is known by it of the corruption of describing [Him] with injustice, foolishness, and lying are two aspects: one of them is the ugliness of that in minds intuitively and through thought, such that it only increases in ugliness upon reflection and investigation, and only increases in foulness upon prolonged consideration. And that is not like the naturally ugly; indeed that becomes good through habit and long practice, like slaughter and its types. And likewise we find the essences of beasts, predators, and birds are instinctively wild towards people, fleeing from what is intended from them of types of gains and works, then they depart from that through training and education until they become familiar with what they used to flee from, and that becomes for them as if it were innate nature. But what is rationally ugly is never in this description; rather it increases with prolonged consideration of its affair. Then, based on that, whoever's action could be that, his promise is not trusted nor his threat feared, nor is his news desired nor his evil safe. And whoever's affair and action is like that, it is impossible to attribute the like of him to the All-Knowing, the Wise by His essence, the Self-Sufficient, with the description that nothing is hidden from Him, nor is any matter difficult for Him when He wills. Rather, according to the Mu'tazilite statement, this cannot be safe from Him, since most things could go out of His will, and what He does not will exists in His domain without His having authority to remove it, because He did not will it. And He wills increase of authority, and He undertakes that to be... and undertakes that to be, but is prevented from that. For example, He wills that all His creation be obedient, but they are not obedient, and He would have in His dominion and kingdom obedience, not disobedience, but it is not. Then He promised people fixed terms for their lives, and He is the one who keeps them, and His promise was to provide them with various provisions until those terms and to send to them types of good things; then a creation from His creation comes and kills them before the term passes, preventing Him from fulfilling what He promised them, by the action that He informed them He would do: preserving their lives until that term. And in that is the necessitation of need and the attachment of lying, which actualize foolishness and injustice, and He has the power over injustice, foolishness, and lying. And every action, if it were, would annul lordship and remove divinity. So they have entered His divinity and lordship under power and management. So when will his state be tranquility of heart by fulfilling what he promised? And there is no power except through Allah. [And] He was described with generosity, kindness, pardon, and beneficence. And in the action by the description with what we mentioned, His exaltation above that and transcendence.
Source pages p. 817

Commentary
This is a complex argument distinguishing rational repugnance from natural repugnance. Maturidi begins by stating that things like lying are never permissible, unlike actions that may be considered foolish or wise depending on circumstances. From a general perspective, foolishness and injustice are impossible for God, but specific actions that humans cannot fully understand should not be labeled as such. He then introduces two aspects that prove the corruption of describing God with injustice, foolishness, or lying. The first aspect (الوجه الأول) is that these qualities are repulsive to the mind both intuitively and through thought, and reflection only increases their ugliness. This is unlike natural repulsiveness (e.g., slaughter or wild animals), which can become acceptable through habit and training. Rationally ugly acts never become acceptable; they only worsen with scrutiny. Therefore, if God were capable of such acts, His promises and threats would not be trustworthy. This would be impossible for the All-Knowing, All-Wise God. Maturidi then criticizes the Mu'tazilite position that God's will can be thwarted, using examples of broken promises and the existence of evil in God's domain, arguing that this would imply need and lying in God, which are incompatible with divinity. He concludes that such attributions would annul lordship and divinity, and God is above that.

Key Terms
قبيح بالعقل
قبيح بالطبع
بديهة
فكر
اعتياد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That something naturally repulsive (like a wild animal) is the same as something rationally repulsive (like lying).
Correction: Maturidi emphasizes that natural repugnance can change through habit, but rational repugnance is immutable and always evil.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what happens to the ugliness of something that is rationally repulsive upon prolonged reflection?
A) It decreases
B) It remains the same
C) It increases
D) It becomes good through habit

Key Takeaway
Rational ugliness is immutable and never becomes good, unlike natural repugnance which can change through habit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified a sin by saying 'it's become a habit' or 'everyone does it'? Reflect on how Maturidi's distinction exposes the fallacy that rational ugliness cannot become good through repetition. What would it mean to stop excusing a rationally ugly action in your life today?
?
The passage says rational ugliness only increases in foulness upon prolonged consideration. Think of a small action you've been reluctant to examine. What would happen if you honestly reflected on it? Are you willing to bring it into the light and name it for what it truly is?
?
Maturidi uses the example of taming wild animals to show natural repugnance changes. Where in your heart have you trained yourself to accept what is actually rationally ugly—perhaps a prejudice, a harsh word, or a negligence? How can you realign your habits with the immutable good that God commands?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
4 min
Implications of Rational Repugnance for God's Attributes
Maturidi argues that rational repugnance disqualifies a being from trustworthiness, so it cannot apply to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing rationally repugnant actions to God would undermine trust in His promises and threats, and why it is impossible for God to have such attributes.

Core Idea
If God could do rationally ugly acts, His promises would be unreliable—but God is All-Knowing and Wise.

After establishing that rational ugliness is immutable, Maturidi draws a critical implication: any being capable of such acts would be untrustworthy. Since God is the All-Knowing, All-Wise, it is impossible that He could commit rationally ugly actions. This lesson traces that logical inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the short passage
Read the passage carefully. Notice how Maturidi links the possibility of committing rationally ugly acts directly to untrustworthiness.
2
Identify the conditional argument
The passage states: if someone's action could be rationally repugnant, then his promise is not trusted, his threat not feared, etc. This is a conditional statement.
Identify the 'if' and 'then' parts.
3
Apply to God
Maturidi then argues that such a being cannot be attributed to the All-Knowing, All-Wise God. Ask yourself: why does the conditional lead to this conclusion?
Because God is described as knowing everything and being self-sufficient.
4
Reflect on the attributes
Note the attributes mentioned: All-Knowing, Wise by essence, Self-Sufficient, nothing hidden from Him. These make it impossible for God to have rationally ugly attributes.

لذلك لزم وصف الله تعالى فى الجملة بالتعالى عن فعل السفه والجور وفى الإشارة أيضا . لكن لا يجوز أن يوصف فيما ظهر فعله بالسفه والجور بما لا يبلغه علم البشر ولا يدركه عقل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم جملة ما يعلم به فساد الوصف بالجور والسفه والكذب وجهان : أحدهما قبح ذلك في العقول بالبديهة والفكر جميعا ، حتى لا يزداد عند التأمل والبحث عنه إلا قبحا ، ولا عند طول النظر فيه إلا فحشا ، وليس ذلك كالقبيح بالطبع ، إن ذلك يصير حسنا بالاعتياد وطول الصحة كالذبح وأنواع ذلك ، وكذلك نجد جواهر الدواب والسباع والطيور مستوحشة عن الناس بالطباع ، نافرة عما يراد بها من أنواع المكاسب والأعمال ، ثم تخرج عنها بالرياضة والتعليم حتى يألف بالذي كان تنفر عنه ، ويصير ذلك له كأنه الطباع المجبول ، ولا يكون الذي قبح بالعقل بهذا الوصف أبدا ، بل يزداد على طول النظر في شأنه . ثم على ذلك من احتمل فعله ذلك لا يوثق لوعده ولا يخاف وعيده ، ولا يرغب في خبره ولا يؤمن شره ، ومن ذا شأنه وعمله فمحال احتمال إضافة مثله إلى العليم الحكيم بذاته الغنى بنفسه ، مع الوصف بأن لا يخفى عليه شيء ، ولا يصعب عليه أمر فيها أراد : ، بل على قول المعتزلة لا يؤمن منه هذا ؛ إذ قد تخرج أكثر الأشياء عن إرادته ، ويوجد ما لا يريده فى سلطانه منه بلا سلطان له في الإخراج عنه ؛ إذ لم يرده ، ويريد زيادة سلطان ، ويتولى ذلك أن يكون ويتولى ذلك أن يكون ، فيمنع عن ذلك ، نحو ما يريد أن يكون جميع خلقه مطيعين ، خلقه مطيعين ، ويكون له فى سلطانه وملكه الطاعة لا المعاصي فلا يكون ، ثم قد كان وعد لقوم مُدَدًا لأعمارهم ، وهو المبقى لهم ، وكان في وعده أن يرزقهم Divine Attributes

Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Position on God's Will
Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazilite argument by showing it leads to absurd consequences about God's power and promises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of the Mu'tazilite view that God's will can be thwarted, and how this leads to attributing need and lying to God, which is impossible.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilite view forces God into a position of unmet promises, implying need and lying—impossible for the Almighty.

Maturidi now takes on a specific opposing view: that of the Mu'tazilites, who held that many things occur contrary to God's will. Using the example of broken promises, Maturidi shows that this would make God subject to need and lying, which are rationally repugnant and cannot be attributed to God. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
Maturidi describes the Mu'tazilite view: things can exist in God's dominion that He does not will, and He is powerless to remove them. This implies God's will is not always effective.
2
Examine the example of promises
Maturidi gives a concrete example: God promises people a fixed lifespan, but then someone kills them prematurely, preventing God from fulfilling the promise. Identify the problem this creates.
3
Identify the consequences
Maturidi argues that this would imply need (الحاجة) and lying (الكذب) in God, which actualize foolishness and injustice. These are rationally repugnant and would annul lordship and divinity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that God is exalted above such attributes. The Mu'tazilite position is thus refuted because it leads to absurdities.

Have you ever felt that God's promises in the Qur'an were delayed or unfulfilled in your life? How does this lesson's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim challenge you to reinterpret those experiences and trust in God's absolute ability to fulfill His word? What will you do the next time doubt creeps in about God's promises?
?
This passage shows that attributing unmet promises to God implies need and lying in Him. Examine your own heart: are there any situations where you have, even subconsciously, thought that a human action could override God's will and cause Him to fail to deliver what He promised? How does the reality of God's perfect power and truthfulness demand a change in your daily reliance on Him?
?
Maturidi says that if God's will could be thwarted, it would annul His lordship and divinity. Consider your actions today: do you live as if your choices truly have power to resist God's will, or do you submit utterly to His sovereignty? Which specific area of your life needs a deeper surrender to the fact that nothing occurs outside His will?

The Second Aspect: Need and Ignorance as Causes
Maturidi begins the second aspect showing that need and ignorance prompt foolish actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the absence of need and ignorance in God to argue against the possibility of divine injustice.

Core Idea
Need and ignorance drive evil; God is free from both, so He is beyond evil actions.

In this lesson, we examine the second aspect of Maturidi's argument that God cannot be described with injustice or foolishness. The passage states that such actions arise solely from need and ignorance—two qualities entirely absent from the Divine. Understanding this link is key to grasping Maturidi's rational proof of God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the concise structure: Maturidi states a causal relationship in one sentence.
The Arabic is short but dense; every word carries weight.
2
Identify the causal claim
The passage says 'that which calls to those actions and incites them is need and ignorance.' Identify what 'those actions' refers to from context—likely injustice, foolishness, and lying mentioned earlier.
3
Reflect on why this proves God's transcendence
Since need and ignorance are the driving forces behind evil actions, and God is utterly free from need and ignorance, He cannot be described with such actions. This is a rational proof of God's perfection.
Think about how this argument fits into broader kalam discussions.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Some might think that only foolishness arises from need and ignorance. But Maturidi applies this to all evil actions collectively. Discuss why this universal scope is important.
Refer to the misconception section for clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثانى أن الذى يدعو إلى تلك الأفعال ويبعث عليها الحاجة والجهل
And the second aspect: that which calls to those actions and incites them is need and ignorance.
Source pages p. 818

Commentary
This passage introduces the second aspect (الوجه الثاني) that demonstrates the impossibility of attributing injustice, foolishness, or lying to God. It states that what drives such actions and motivates them is need (حاجة) and ignorance (جهل). Since God is free from need and ignorance, He cannot be described with such actions. This is a concise statement that sets up the argument, but the detailed elaboration is not present in the provided pages.

Key Terms
وجه ثان
حاجة
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that only foolishness is caused by need and ignorance, while other evil actions have different causes.
Correction: Maturidi states that need and ignorance are what call to 'those actions' (injustice, foolishness, lying) collectively, implying they are the universal root of all such evil acts.

Checkpoint
According to the second aspect mentioned by Maturidi, what are the two causes that call to actions like injustice and foolishness?
A) Power and control
B) Need and ignorance
C) Habit and nature
D) Wisdom and knowledge

Key Takeaway
Need and ignorance are the roots of evil actions; God, being free of both, is absolutely transcendent above such flaws.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you act out of need (e.g., for approval, wealth, control) or ignorance, do you see the evil it produces in your life? How can you turn to God's self-sufficiency rather than fueling that need?
?
If God cannot do evil because He lacks need and ignorance, what does that say about your own need to constantly examine your motives? Are there areas where you excuse your sin because you feel justified by a 'need'?
?
Imagine standing before God and being asked about an injustice you committed. Would your excuse be 'I needed it' or 'I didn't know'? How does Maturidi's argument challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions starting today?
