Two Methods of Reasoning from Witnessed to Unseen
Maturidi critiques two methods of analogical reasoning from shahid to gha'ib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's critique of two methods of analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen and why both methods fail.

Core Idea
Analogy from seen to unseen must be based on meaning, not superficial resemblance.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi examines how opponents might try to argue from the created world to the divine. He identifies two possible approaches—direct analogy of human categories and consideration of underlying meaning—and demonstrates that neither can stand. This analysis reveals the importance of proper reasoning in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall argument. Notice how Maturidi structures his critique around two options.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two methods
Find the two methods Maturidi presents: (1) judging everything by human wisdom/foolishness, and (2) considering the underlying meaning that makes something what it is.
These are introduced with 'إما أن يجعل...' and 'أو ينظر...'.
3
Examine the refutation of the first method
Notice the absurd consequences Maturidi lists: creating without benefit, creating from nothing, punishment without cause, and permitting lying. This shows the first method leads to theological problems.
4
Examine the refutation of the second method
Understand why the second method is invalid: it relies on 'intuition of reason' (badahat al-aql), which is not a sound basis and is dismissed as baseless fantasy.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Maturidi's critique matters for tawhid. It shows that reasoning from creation to God must be done carefully, avoiding simplistic analogies.
This connects to the broader methodology of Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا بخروجه ذلك وتعنته فما ادعى ، على أنه لا يخلو من أحد أمرين : إما أن يجعل كل شيء يعرفه فى الشاهد البشر حكمة أو سفها يقول به فى الغائب ، أو ينظر إلى المعنى الذي له صار كذلك من في التحقيق فنقول به فى الغائب، فإن قال بالأول لزمه ذلك في خلق ما لا ينتفع به ، وفى خلق الشيء من لا شيء ، وفى التعذيب من غير دفع ، ثم يقال نفسه في إجازته قوله بالكذب ، فمها أجاب من شيء فذلك لازم له فيما قال ، وإن نظر إلى المعنى أبطل قوله ذا يبديهة العقل ، وذا لا يجوز له ، وهذا النوع من الخيال الذي لا أصل له فلا
And we have shown through his deviation and stubbornness what he claimed, that he is not free from one of two matters: either he makes everything he knows in the witnessed (al-shahid) of man wisdom or foolishness and says it about the unseen (al-gha'ib), or he considers the meaning by which it became so from verification and say it about the unseen. If he says the first, then it necessitates for him in creating what is not beneficial, and in creating a thing from nothing, and in punishment without repelling, then he says himself in permitting his statement of lying, so whatever he answers from something, that is necessary for him regarding what he said. And if he considers the meaning, he invalidates his statement by intuition of reason (badahat al-aql), and that is not permissible for him. And this type of fantasy that has no foundation, so no.
Source pages p. 866

Commentary
This passage is a critique of two possible methods of analogical reasoning from the witnessed (shahid) to the unseen (gha'ib). Maturidi states that any opponent must adopt one of two approaches: either they judge everything in the unseen by direct analogy of wisdom or foolishness as seen in humans, or they consider the underlying meaning (al-ma'na) that makes a thing what it is in the witnessed and then apply that to the unseen. He then refutes both. The first leads to absurd consequences: it would necessitate that God creates things without benefit, creates from nothing, and punishes without cause, and it also forces the opponent to permit lying (al-kadhib). Whatever answer they give, it remains binding. The second approach is invalid because it relies on 'intuition of reason' (badahat al-aql), which is not a valid basis for judgment—such intuitive claims are baseless fantasy. Maturidi thus shows that neither method can stand.

Key Terms
الشاهد (al-shahid)
الغائب (al-gha'ib)
الحكمة (al-hikma)
السفه (al-safah)
بديهة العقل (badahat al-aql)
المعنى (al-ma'na)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Analogical reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen is always valid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that it must be carefully critiqued; both naive analogy and appeal to underlying meaning are flawed.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the two possible methods for reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen?
A) Analogy of essence and analogy of accident
B) Analogy of wisdom/foolishness and consideration of underlying meaning
C) Analogy of benefit and harm
D) Analogy of power and weakness

Key Takeaway
Maturidi demonstrates that neither direct analogy nor intuitive meaning can justify reasoning from creation to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or see suffering in the world, do you find yourself quietly measuring God's wisdom by your own? How does this passage expose that tendency as absurd?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something makes sense to your intuition, it must be true about God? Maturidi calls such intuition a baseless fantasy. What assumptions about God do you hold that you have never critically examined?
?
This passage forces you to choose: will you continue to project your own ideas of wisdom and foolishness onto God, or will you submit to the realization that God's ways are beyond your comprehension? What practical step can you take this week to guard against such projection?

Refutation of the Objection on Creating Non-Beneficial Things
Maturidi rebuts the objection that creating non-beneficial things is ugly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that God's creation of non-beneficial things does not entail ugliness or obligation, and that ugliness is not intrinsic to actions like obscenities and disbelief.

Core Idea
Ugliness is not intrinsic; benefit is not incumbent upon the Creator.

This passage addresses a key objection: if God creates things with no apparent benefit, does that imply ugliness? Maturidi refutes this by showing that the opponent's appeal to 'intuition of reason' is unfounded, and that what is considered ugly (like obscenities and disbelief) is not ugly in itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get an overview of the argument. Note the flow from objection to refutation.
2
Identify the main objection and response
What is the objection? It is about creating non-beneficial things. How does Maturidi respond? He says it does not entail obligation.
3
Examine the intuition of reason claim
Why does Maturidi say the opponent's claim is false? Because they are only using analogy, not pure intuition.
4
Understand the nature of ugliness
What does Maturidi conclude about ugly actions? They are not ugly in themselves. This is a key theological point.
5
Reflect on benefit and harm
How does the concept of benefit relate to wisdom? Actions not beneficial to themselves are not described as wisdom; but insistence on benefit can become harmful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تم دفع ما عورض بخلق شيء لا ينتفع به بما لم يدل ذلك على تكليف . الزمين ، فهذا يبين أن الذى قال ببديهة العقل كذب ، وأنه إنما ادعى القياس . على ما وافقه خصمه عليه لا غير ، ثم حصل بما عارض به خصمه مما ادعى ببديهة العقل على من يثبت صدقا مما هو كذب في الشاهد . ثم أجاب بما اختراز النفع بالفعل لا يحق عليه ، ثبت أن ما قبح منه لم يقبح لعينه ، وكذلك يجد خصمه مما يقدر عليه لا يجوز التكليف به ، ثبت أن ذلك لم يقبح لنفسه نحو الفواحش والكفر ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . يوجود وذا أولى لما يجئ مثل هذا من الصغار لا يلحقه وصف فحش ولا كفر ، ولا يجئ منهم فعل غير نافع نفسه يوصف بالحكمة ، والذى قال من إصرار النفع فهو من ذلك الوجه حكمة ، ولكن من وجه الضرر صار كذلك ، وهو ضرر العاقبة أو كفران النعمة أو مخالفة الرب في الفعل. وإذا كان به دفعه ولزمه السؤال ولم يلزم خصمه فيما عارضه بالزمن الذى أجابه مما ينقض عليه ليعلم به بعده عن الحق فيها يوافق عليه ويخالف فيه جميعا . ثم ان جواب خصمه سهل ، وهو ما بينا ، والله أعلم .
Then was refuted what was objected regarding creating something not beneficial, by that not indicating obligation upon the two times [؟]. This clarifies that the one who said by intuition of reason (badahat al-aql) lied, and that he only claimed analogy upon what his opponent agreed with him, nothing else. Then he obtained by what he opposed his opponent from what he claimed by intuition of reason against one who affirms truth from what is false in the witnessed. Then he answered by selecting benefit by the act is not incumbent upon him. It is established that what is ugly from it is not ugly in itself, and likewise his opponent finds from what he has power over that obligation is not permissible by it. It is established that that is not ugly in itself, like obscenities (al-fawahish) and disbelief (al-kufr). And there is no power except with God. And it exists, and this is more appropriate for what comes like this from small actions that do not admit description of obscenity nor disbelief, and no action not beneficial to itself comes from them that is described as wisdom. And what he said of insistence on benefit is from that aspect wisdom, but from the aspect of harm it becomes thus, and it is harm of the outcome or ingratitude for blessing or disobedience to the Lord in action. And if it is by that, he refutes it and the question is necessary for him, and it is not necessary for his opponent in what he opposed him with the time he answered, from what invalidates his argument, so that by it he knows his distance from the truth in what he agrees upon and differs in all. Then the answer of his opponent is easy, and it is what we have shown, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning God's creation of non-beneficial things. Maturidi argues that such creation does not entail any obligation (taklif) on God. This shows that the opponent who claims 'intuition of reason' is lying; they are merely claiming analogy based on what their opponent has already accepted. The opponent attempts to use intuitive reason to establish that something true in the witnessed is false, but Maturidi counters that the benefit of an act is not necessarily incumbent upon the agent. It is then established that what is ugly (qabih) from God is not ugly in its essence (li-aynihi), just as the opponent himself finds that obligation is not permissible for what he has power over—thus obscenities (fawahish) and disbelief (kufr) are not ugly in themselves. Maturidi further notes that small actions that are not classed as obscene or disbelief and that are not beneficial to themselves are not described as wisdom. The claim of 'insistence on benefit' is wisdom from one aspect but becomes harmful from another—harm of outcome, ingratitude, or disobedience. The opponent's objection is refuted, and the answer is straightforward.

Key Terms
بديهة العقل (badahat al-aql)
التكليف (al-taklif)
القبيح بعينه (al-qabih bi-aynihi)
الفواحش (al-fawahish)
الكفر (al-kufr)
الحكمة (al-hikma)
الضرر (al-darar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creating non-beneficial things is intrinsically ugly and thus impossible for God.
Correction: Maturidi argues that ugliness is not intrinsic; what is ugly in human context (like obscenities) is not ugly in itself. God's creation of non-beneficial things does not entail obligation or ugliness.

Checkpoint

A) They are ugly in themselves (li-aynihi)
B) They are not ugly in themselves
C) They are ugly only if they cause harm
D) They are ugly because reason deems them so

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the objection that God's creation of non-beneficial things is ugly, showing that ugliness is not intrinsic and that benefit is not incumbent upon the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged an action as inherently evil without considering its context or outcome? Maturidi teaches that ugliness is not intrinsic. How does this challenge your habit of feeling self-righteous about your own actions? Will you now pause before condemning others, recognizing that God alone knows the full reality?
?
You claim to trust God's wisdom, yet you often question His plans when they bring hardship. This lesson shows that benefit is not incumbent upon God and that harm may have purpose. ?
Maturidi refutes the idea that ugliness is intrinsic. Think of a sin you despise—does your hatred stem from its true nature or from your own subjective perspective? How can you align your moral judgments with divine wisdom, not just your feelings? What step will you take today to check your assumptions about good and evil?

The Wisdom of Giving to a Disobedient Servant
Maturidi addresses the objection that giving ability to a servant who will disobey is unwise, and refutes the Mu'tazila claim regarding providing power for faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that giving power to a disobedient servant is unwise, and why God's gifts can serve purposes beyond immediate obedience.

Core Idea
Gifts can be wise even when the recipient disobeys.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection-and-answer concerning divine wisdom. The opponent argues that it would be foolish for a master to give resources to a servant who will use them to disobey. Maturidi responds by distinguishing the purpose of the gift: it may serve other benefits, not merely faith. He then refutes the Mu'tazila who claim God gives power specifically for belief, knowing it will lead to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an objection, a counter-objection, a response, a refutation of the Mu'tazila, and a final reaffirmation.
The passage begins with 'ثم قال' and ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'.
2
Identify the initial objection
Find where Maturidi presents the objection: giving something beneficial to one who will disobey is considered ugly. Also note the counter-objection about giving to someone incapable.
3
Understand Maturidi's response
Maturidi uses an analogy: one may feed a messenger even if knowing he won't believe, because the feeding is for other benefits. Similarly, God may give power not for belief but for other wise purposes.
The key is that the purpose of the gift determines its wisdom.
4
Reflect on the Mu'tazila refutation
Maturidi rejects the Mu'tazila claim that God gives power specifically so the person may believe, while knowing he will disbelieve. That would be impossible and not considered wise.
This shows that Maturidi defends God's wisdom without implying futility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : ذلك الذي قيل فيمن يفعل الحاجة . قيل : والأول أيضا قبح ممن لا يملك التقوية لو طلب منه وتضرع إليه . ثم عارض نفسه بمن يدفع إلى عبده ما يعلم يعصيه به فقال : قد يكون ذلك حكمة نحو من يعلم بخير الرسول أنه لا يؤمن ، يجوز أن يطعمه ونحو ذلك مما يعلم من تأمله جهله بما عارض به نفسه ؛ لأن الذي ذكره لا يجوز أن يعطيه ليؤمن بذلك بعد علمه بأنه لا يفعل ، وإنما يعطيه لمنافع سوى هذا . والمعتزلة تزعم أنه أعطى القوة ليؤمن بها ، وهو يعلم أنه يكفر بها ، فليس ذلك مما قدر فى شيء . ثم المعارضة كانت فيمن يعصيه ، فلا أحد يعد نفسه فى الحكماء إذا علم أن عبده بالذى يعطيه يعصيه ولا يكتسب رضاه بل يعمل بعداوته وشتمه ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Then he said: That which was said about one who does a need. It was said: And the first also is ugly from one who does not have the ability to strengthen if asked and implored. Then he opposed himself regarding one who gives to his servant what he knows he will disobey him with, and said: That may be wisdom, like one who knows of the goodness of the messenger that he will not believe, may be permissible to feed him, and similar to that which he knows from his reflection, his ignorance of what he opposed himself with; because what he mentioned is not permissible to give to him so that he may believe thereby after knowing that he will not do so, but he gives it for benefits besides this. And the Mu'tazila claim that he gave the power so that he may believe by it, while He knows that he will disbelieve by it. That is not something considered at all. Then the objection was about one who disobeys Him, so no one counts himself among the wise if he knows that his servant, by what he gives him, disobeys him and does not acquire his pleasure, but rather acts with enmity and reviling of him. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the wisdom of providing means to one who will disobey. Maturidi presents an opponent's objection: if giving something beneficial to one who will use it to disobey is considered ugly, then it would also be ugly to give to someone incapable even if they ask. But Maturidi then responds by considering the case of giving to a servant whom the master knows will disobey. He argues that such giving may still be wisdom (hikma), analogous to feeding someone whom one knows will not believe—the feeding is not for the purpose of belief but for other benefits. He then refutes the Mu'tazila claim that God gives power (al-quwwa) specifically so that the person may believe, while knowing they will disbelieve; Maturidi considers this impossible. Finally, he reaffirms that no wise person would give to a servant who will use the gift to disobey and revile him. Thus, the objection is answered by distinguishing the purpose of the gift.

Key Terms
الحاجة (al-haja)
التقوية (al-taqwiya)
الحكمة (al-hikma)
المعتزلة (al-mu'tazila)
القوة (al-quwwa)
الكفر (al-kufr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God giving power to a disbeliever is futile because the person will not believe.
Correction: Maturidi explains that God's gifts may serve other benefits beyond faith; the purpose is not always the specific act of belief.

Checkpoint
What is the Mu'tazila claim that Maturidi refutes in this passage?
A) God gives power for no reason
B) God gives power specifically so that the person may believe, even if He knows they will disbelieve
C) God gives power only to those who will obey
D) God does not give power to disbelievers

Key Takeaway
God's giving of power or provisions is not necessarily for the purpose of causing faith; it may serve other wise benefits even if the recipient disobeys.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a blessing from Allah while you are in a state of disobedience, what do you assume about His wisdom? Do you think He is unaware or that He is giving you a chance for repentance, or do you feel entitled? How does this lesson challenge your assumption? What will you change in your attitude today?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that Allah gives power specifically for faith, but Maturidi refutes that. Have you ever felt that a particular trial or gift was given to you for a specific purpose that you didn't fulfill? How does understanding that Allah's gifts may have multiple purposes affect your gratitude and your response to His blessings? What concrete step will you take to use your abilities for obedience despite past failures?
?
Imagine you are the servant in the analogy: you have been given ability, but you use it to disobey. Do you realize that the gift is still from Allah for other benefits? How should that change your attitude towards repenting and using your abilities for obedience? Are you willing to apologize to Allah and start afresh, or will you continue in ingratitude? Make a decision now.

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Children and the Absence of Ugliness
Maturidi explains that children's actions are not described as ugly or kufr, and discusses the criteria for wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why children's actions are not classified as immoral or disbelief, and how actions are evaluated as wise or harmful based on benefit and consequence.

Core Idea
Children's actions lack moral categories; only actions with capacity receive such labels.

Maturidi addresses a key point in Islamic theology: that moral and religious descriptors like 'immorality' or 'disbelief' do not apply to children's actions. This passage establishes the foundational principle that such judgments require capacity and awareness. Additionally, it explains how actions are assessed as wisdom (benefit) or harm, and touches on a dialectical argument about consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Notice how Maturidi states his claim about children and then moves to the criteria for wisdom.
The Arabic has some textual variants noted by the editor, but the core meaning is clear.
2
Identify the key claim about children
The passage explicitly states that no description of immorality (fuhsh) or disbelief (kufr) attaches to actions that come from small children. Ask yourself: why is this important?
This connects to the wider issue of moral responsibility and the absence of taklif for children.
3
Understand the criteria for wisdom and harm
Maturidi explains that an action is considered wisdom from the aspect of benefit, but harm from the aspect of harm in the consequence, ingratitude, or opposing the Lord. Reflect on how this relates to the classification of actions.
The dialectical part about the opponent is supplementary; focus on the main principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذا أولى لما يجئ مثل هذا من الصغار لا يلحقه وصف فحش ولا كفر، ولا يجئ منهم فعل غير نافع نفسه يوصف بالحكمة، والذي قال من إصرار النفع فهو من ذلك الوجه حكمة، ولكن من وجه الضرر صار كذلك، وهو ضرر العاقبة أو كفران النعمة أو مخالفة الرب في الفعل. وإذا كان به دفع ولزمه السؤال ولم يلزم خصمه فيما عارضه بالزمن الذي أجابه مما ينقض عليه ليعلم به بعده عن الحق فيها يوافق عليه ويخالف فيه جميعا.
And this is more appropriate, because such [actions] come from small children: no description of immorality or disbelief attaches to them, and there does not come from them an act that is not beneficial in itself that is described as wisdom. And that which is said about insistence on benefit, it is from that aspect wisdom, but from the aspect of harm it becomes so, and that is harm of consequence, or ingratitude for blessing, or opposing the Lord in action. And if it is repelled by him, the question becomes necessary for him, but not for his opponent in what he objected to him in the time[s] he answered, that which invalidates him, so that he may know by it his distance from the truth in that which he agrees upon and disagrees upon altogether.
Source pages p. 867

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory definition of how actions are assessed. Maturidi states that children's actions do not receive the moral descriptors 'immorality' or 'disbelief' because they lack the capacity for such evaluation. He then discusses the attribution of 'wisdom' (hikmah) to an action: it is considered good if it brings benefit, and harmful if it leads to future harm, ingratitude, or disobedience. The passage also addresses a dialectical point: when an opponent raises an objection based on comparing the witnessed (al-shahid) to the unseen (al-gha'ib), Maturidi notes that the objection does not hold consistently because the opponent himself agrees and disagrees selectively, demonstrating his distance from truth.

Key Terms
الصغار
الفحش
الكفر
الحكمة
الضرر
العاقبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That children are considered sinful or that their actions can be described as immoral or disbelief.
Correction: The passage clearly states that such descriptions do not attach to children's actions because they lack the capacity for moral and religious assessment.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are children's actions not described as immoral or disbelief?
A) Because children are always obedient.
B) Because children lack the capacity for such descriptions.
C) Because children's actions are always beneficial.
D) Because children are not responsible for their actions.

Key Takeaway
Children's actions are not classified as immoral or disbelief, and actions are evaluated as wise if beneficial, harmful if they lead to harm or ingratitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged a child's action as morally wrong, forgetting that they lack the capacity for such labels? How must you change the way you react to children's mistakes after reading this lesson?
?
This lesson warns that insistence on benefit can become harmful if it leads to ingratitude or disobedience to Allah. In what area of your life are you prioritizing short-term gain over obeying Allah, and what harm of consequence are you risking?
?
The dialectical argument exposes inconsistency in the opponent. Where in your own life do you apply Islamic principles selectively—agreeing in some cases and disagreeing in others—thus distancing yourself from the truth? Commit to one specific change.
